Master of Science in Civil Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark. The
project is credited to 35 ECTS points. The theme of the project was decided by the
Author, however, small adjustments have been made during the process in collabora-

==PAGE:8==
==PAGE:9==
Abstract
The use of computational automation in the building industry is only sparsely intro-
duced compared to other industries. The building industry has kept the usage of hand
calculation and spreadsheets as the primary tools, making design changes and itera-
tions diﬃcult and tiresome to perform. The inability to create adaptive calculations
lead to buildings being designed without veriﬁcation, as the initiation of calculations
happen after the early design phase has ended. This increase the risk of expensive
design changes and delayed projects in addition to a generally hostile division between
the designers, architects, engineers and contractors.
The aim of this thesis is to study the possibilities of developing a computational
method of introducing an automated intelligent optimization of steel structures in the
early design phase. The motivation for this is an easement of communication and ﬂexi-
bility for the engineer and the ability to automatically evaluate and calculating multiple
designs aiding the design choices going forward by presenting designers, engineers, and
contractors with early indications of promising design paths without limiting the free-
dom of design.
The outcome of this thesis is a tool able to perform optimization of both Revit
models and Robot Structural Analysis models through the visual programming tool
Dynamo. The driver of the optimization is the genetic algorithm NSGA-II making
the iterations more intelligent working towards an optimal solution faster than conven-
tional methods. The tool requires either an analytically correct 3D BIM Revit model
as deﬁned by (Hejnfelt, 2016) or a likewise calculate-able Robot model. The tool can
take a static model and perform changes to key elements and adaptions on the topol-
ogy of a structure. The results are subsequently automatically visualized in a parallel
coordinate plot using an interactive web-page format (.html), created through an inde-
pendent Python script, allowing for a quick overview of all generated solutions.
From this study, it is evident that it is possible to create automatic optimization
tools aiding decision making in the early design phase. Thus working towards more
intelligent structures with a decreased risk of expensive design changes eﬀectively nar-
rowing the gap between designers, architects, and engineers.

==PAGE:10==
==PAGE:11==
Resume - Dansk
Computerdrevet automatisering er i dag kun brugt i begrænsede mængder i byggein-
dustrien, sammenlignet med andre brancher. Byggeindustrien har således bibeholdt
brugen af regneark og hånd-beregninger som primære beregningsværktøjer, hvilket
besværliggør projektændringer og iterationer. Dette har betydet, at bygninger ofte
bliver designet og projekteret uden brugen af beregninger, da beregningsfasen først
igangsættes, efter det endelige design er blevet fastlagt. Dette kan resultere i dyre de-
signændringer og forsinkede projekter samt et usamarbejdsvilligt arbejdsmiljø mellem
arkitekter og ingeniører.
Formålet med denne afhandling er at undersøge mulighederne for at udvikle en com-
puterdrevet metode til automatiserede og intelligent optimerede stål-konstruktioner i
de tidlige designfaser. Motivationen for dette er muligheden for at lette kommunika-
tionen og ﬂeksibiliteten for ingeniøren samt muliggøre at evaluere og beregne en stor
mængde forskellige designmuligheder.
Dette er gjort for at lette valgprocessen ved
at præsentere ingeniører og designere for en tidlig indikation af lovende løsningsmu-
ligheder uden at fastlåse projektet eller miste designfrihed.
Resultatet af denne afhandling er et værktøj, der muliggør en optimering af både
Revit- samt Robot Structural Analysis-modeller vha. visuel programmering igennem
værktøjet Dynamo. Optimeringsprocessen drives af den genetiske algoritme NSGA-II,
der baner vejen for en mere intelligent udvælgelsesmetode hen mod en optimal løsning,
sammenlignet med konventionelle metoder. Værktøjet kræver en korrekt analytisk 3D
Revit BIM-model som deﬁneret af (Hejnfelt, 2016) eller en beregningsklar Robot-model.
Værktøjet kan tage en statisk model og foretage ændringer på de vigtigste elementers
tværsnit samt foretage ændringer af modellens topologi.
Resultaterne kan herefter
automatisk visualiseres i et parallelt koordinatplot ved brug af et interaktivt format
(.html) genereret med en særstående Python-kode. Herigennem kan brugeren opnå et
hurtigt visuelt overblik over de genererede løsningsforslag og eventuelle konsekvenser
ved de individuelle løsninger.
Ud fra denne afhandling kan det konkluderes, at det er muligt at udarbejde et
værktøj der kan foretage automatisk optimering og derved bistå beslutningsprocessen
i de tidlige designfaser. Der kan herved arbejdes hen imod mere intelligente bygninger
med lavere risiko for dyre designændringer. Dette er aktivt med til at bistå et frugtbart
samarbejde mellem designere, arkitekter og ingeniører.

CHAPTER 1
Introduction
The idea of Building Information Modelling (BIM) in the construction industry has
been the topic on everybody’s lips for the past 10-15 years in the industry. The idea of
automatic data exchange and re-utilization has been introduced in several areas within
the Architecture, Engineering, and Construction (AEC) industry. The eﬃciency of
reusing the information is, however, still not optimal as analysis and optimization hap-
pen too late to have any real inﬂuence on the design (Cavieres, Gentry, and Al-Haddad,
2011) this can result in expensive project changes and reduced solution space.
On average, 2.7 diﬀerent designs of a building or system is considered. This is caused
by limited time and the expense connected with exploring design alternatives in the
early design phase (Biilmann, 2015). By introducing automation into the early design
phase, an estimation predicts that the number of model alternatives evaluated can be
multiplied by at least a factor 1000 according to Biilmann. The increase in evaluated
designs gives rise to a more optimized early model which caters to the clients’ needs,
while at the same time reduces the risk of expensive design changes, by taking early
decisions based on more information.
The focus of this thesis is the possibility of developing tools which can utilize the early
BIM model and execute preliminary structural analysis utilizing genetic algorithms for
generating and delivering optimized proposals to the user.
The most optimal model is most often a matter of perspective, depending on the back-
ground, and interests of the observer. As a result of this, the presentation of several
optimized variances should be the foundation for decisions and the work carried out in
this thesis should focus on aiding without dictating decisions.
The project caters to the mindset behind Virtual Design and Construction (VDC) and
BIM by optimizing repetitive tasks and thus free up time for the architects and engi-
neers for solving other intricate problems and create more spectacular projects (Addis,
2007).
In this chapter, the problem statement is presented along with the methodology show-
ing the approach towards solving the challenges put forth in the problem statement.
The identiﬁcation of areas of intervention is determined using a mixture of a literary
study and interviews discovering the most promising area and thus drive the future
analysis. Firstly, the motivation to why this project has been deemed interesting for
investigation.
1
==PAGE:20==
2
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.1
Motivation
In recent years an increased focus and investment towards digitalization and central-
ization of information is forming within the AEC industry. Most companies have an
interest in utilizing the opportunities which BIM and VDC bring with them (Biilmann,
2015), but currently, only a fraction of the possibilities are realized.
The issue largely stems from the fact that most projects are evaluated and iterated
only a few times which result in a non-optimal structure and a risk of expensive design
changes. The reason for this lack of optimization is the amount of time required to
perform remodelling, analysis, and evaluations of diﬀerent and varying design options.
A study conducted by Matousek and Schneider, 1976 shows that 36% of structural
failures comes from insuﬃcient knowledge while 13% comes from forgetfulness. By
automating the evaluation process, it is believed that more alternatives can be evalu-
ated giving a better understanding and knowledge of the design. It is believed that by
introducing automation of tedious processes it is possible to combat forgetfulness and
assist in decision making.
When an analysis is carried out later in the process, changes to the design are expen-
sive and time-consuming (Pedersen, 2006). There is thus a motivation towards using
Visual Programming (VP) for semi-automated work-ﬂows carrying out initial analysis
and ranking of several design-alternatives resulting in a potential for reduced cost, time,
material usage, and number of errors.
1.2
Problem Statement
The need for more optimized and performance eﬃcient buildings is an ever-rising chal-
lenge for companies. Those who can adapt to this increasing demand and the rising
involvement of the client early in the design phase are leading the evolution (Biilmann,
2015). An industrialization is currently washing over the building industry, the in-
troduction of IT related tools drives productivity and visualization to unprecedented
levels.
This brings forth the problem statement for this thesis:
How can several solution alternatives be created and evaluated for the
structural design in the early design phase using Visual Programming as a
primary enabler
The motivation for this thesis is the development of a work-ﬂow which can both auto-
mate and optimize the evaluation of an early design model. This process is assessed
most eﬀective by a combination of VP and regular programming as the primary tools.
By automating the evaluation process, it is possible to reduce costs (Pedersen, 2006) as
well as the risk of human errors. The optimization serves to evaluate a wider solution
space and free up resources to work on other challenging problems.
Using the VP tool Dynamo which can interact with Revit and Robot Structural Analy-
sis (RSA) through the Application Programming Interface (API), it is deemed possible
to capture the geometry and translate this to a dynamic model which can be automati-
cally analysed and optimized. More information about this can be found in Section 2.4.
==PAGE:21==
1.3. METHODOLOGY
3
The Finite element method (FEM) model can further be utilized to create initial struc-
tural calculations, generate proposals for the necessary column spacing and estimate
the dimensions of sections.
The optimization procedure proposed will focus on creating Multi-Objective Optimiza-
tion (MOO), more information regarding MOO can be found in Section 2.5.
Finally, a discussion of model maturity, necessary information and the need for a cer-
tain Level Of Information (LOI) is performed.
To limit the scope of this thesis, the focus will lie within the ﬁeld of structural en-
gineering and steel structures. The areas to be optimized upon are found based on a
literary study, case studies, and interviews with industry professionals. The developed
work-ﬂow will subsequently be presented to the interviewees verifying the relevance of
the developed solution(s).
1.3
Methodology
The purpose of this thesis is to identify and develop a work-ﬂow which can answer and
solve the problem stated and thus optimize upon early building design. To identify
areas of intervention and to generate a foundation for further analysis, a literary study
is carried out evaluating trends and work currently going on in the industry.
To identify the current work-ﬂow several interviews are performed with industry pro-
fessionals to verify the information discovered in the literary study.
Models from the industry partner Sweco is used as case studies to test the developed
VP work-ﬂows and further develop these. These case studies show how the developed
work-ﬂows serves to be used in a real project and the possibilities related to the semi-
automated evaluation of building models.
The thesis focus on retaining a ﬁrm hold in reality and the information available in the
BIM/VDC model and thus re-utilize the data already available. As no predecessors
known to the Author exists, the work is kept at an experimental level. A discussion
of challenges and limitations of the developed work-ﬂow will happen throughout the
thesis.
The work is carried out by developing the optimization graphs in Dynamo and Python
ensuring concurrency, which enables the possibility of performing various kinds of MOO
on several building-types and -complexities. The case structures analysed are a 2D
truss, a complex 3D structure, and a typical steel frame structure.
As the possibility of human error is present while programming veriﬁcation of results is
presented in the form of separate structural analysis, automated visualization of results
and recording of design alternatives.
==PAGE:22==
4
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.4
Literary Study
The information, opinions, and research found in the literary study are explained and
cited in this section.
BIM is the process of generating and managing a digital representation of a build-
ing’s physical and functional characteristics to facilitate an exchange of information
(Eastman et al., 2008). This deﬁnition covers the intend of BIM, but the reality in the
AEC industry is a rather slow adoption rate and most companies to this day relies on
traditional methods (Azhar, 2011), (Gu and London, 2010). One reason might be found
in the allocation of accountability, accuracy of information for shared models, and possi-
ble contractual issues deﬁning responsibilities within the digital models. (Azhar, 2011).
"Creating a BIM makes sense only if software is available to populate the BIM with
data that can be reused by other software, and only if software exists to extract and
import data from a BIM" (Bazjanac, 2005)
The goal of working more eﬃciently together during a building project is clear, the
goal, however, is clearer than the path. One way could be to introduce tools allowing
for automatic evaluation of projects in the early design phase. These evaluations could
open possibilities for a more intelligent design having a more signiﬁcant impact on the
performance of the ﬁnal building (Pedersen, 2006) rather than discovering potential
issues later. Currently, most early design decisions are taken based on experience and
rules of thumb (Rahmani, Stoupine, et al., 2015) rather than based on analysis and
information contradicting the intend of BIM as described earlier. This issue might
stem from the limited ability to integrate and create concurrency in the BIM model in
an early design phase (Cavieres, Gentry, and Al-Haddad, 2011).
Changes made in the early design phase have the most signiﬁcant impact on the over-
all cost as design changes are costly to implement later in the process as indicated by
several studies (Pedersen, 2006), (Cavieres, Gentry, and Al-Haddad, 2011), (Bazjanac,
2005), (Rahmani, Bergin, et al., 2014), (Azhar, 2011).
To achieve the goal of early optimization the tools conducting the analysis needs to be
available and able to interact with both the early BIM model and analysis software.
A general lack of computational tools in the early design phase is evident (Cavieres,
Gentry, and Al-Haddad, 2011). The absence of these tools might stem from the lack
of IT-related abilities within the ﬁeld of AEC. Haymaker, 2006 observes issues within
communication, and integration of BIM, leading to problems of balance, and optimiza-
tion of projects.
The demands for IT abilities from the AEC industry increase with the continued vir-
tualization of projects. As a result the abilities taught at universities are expected to
match the demands set by the industry. The general IT abilities required at a master
level is deﬁned by Menzel, Rebolj, and Turk, 2006 as:
• Use common computer tools to produce documents and make presentations.
• Develop system models to carry out calculations and simulations.
• Design and maintain simple Internet work-space to support interactive teamwork.
==PAGE:23==
1.4. LITERARY STUDY
5
• Computer-based tasks using object-oriented programming and expert systems.
• Use professional computer codes to prepare data, obtain reasonable results from
calculations and to verify results from an "engineering" point of view.
• Understand algorithms, their limitations and requirements, prepare the data for
processing with professional code and analyze obtained results.
With these IT abilities, an engineer can develop computational software supporting
analysis and optimization in the early design phase.
"The next phase in digital design lies in considering the computer as a collaborative
partner in the design process that is capable of generating ideas and stimulating solu-
tions in response to robust and rigorous models of design conditions and performance."
(Shea, Aish, and Gourtovaia, 2005)
Design optimization has yet to reach its full potential as only a limited number of
projects has utilized this approach within the ﬁeld of structural engineering (Tserani-
dis, Brown, and Mueller, 2016). Some tools for the optimization of energy and indoor
environment have been developed utilizing information from models, performing early
analysis of buildings; some worth mentioning are: (Pedersen, 2006), (Bazjanac, 2005),
(Rahmani, Bergin, et al., 2014), (K. M. Kensek, 2014), (Caldas, 2001), (Konis, Gamas,
and K. Kensek, 2016), (Rahmani, Zarrinmehr, et al., 2015).
The general objective of using early-stage analysis is to reduce the time required for
performing detailed analysis and avoid the need for expensive changes in a later phase.
Autodesk has released a tool named Dynamo working within Revit as a visual pro-
gramming tool with access to the Revit API, along with this a few online classes in ex-
perimental structural analysis using this tool is released e.g. (Weyermann, 2015), (Ver-
meulen, 2015), (Vermeulen, 2017a), (Vermeulen, 2017b), (Vermeulen, 2017c). These
classes along with additional learning material work to experiment with structural op-
timization using visual programming as a primary enabler, although the models used
are error-free and thus does not suﬃciently resemble current industry models.
An example of structural optimization can be found in the article by Baldock and
Shea, 2006 optimizing the structural topology of bracing using genetic programming
and the article by Shea, Aish, and Gourtovaia, 2005 optimizing upon the topology of
a cantilever truss.
To have the biggest possible impact, the structural optimization needs to happen early
(Cavieres, Gentry, and Al-Haddad, 2011), while the project is still on the drawing board,
and design changes are cheaper to implement. The impact of decisions can be seen in
Figure 1.1 combining an improved foreground knowledge of the design (Fabrycky and
Blanchard, 1991) with an improved availability of computational tools (L. Wang et al.,
2002) displaying the opportunity of a more integrated early design phase.
Using VP as an enabler for driving models is possible by combining small blocks of code
to form a work-ﬂow which can carry out routine tasks. These VP tools are working
as a surrogate program who can leach information from the main program. Examples
of this is Dynamo getting information from Revit or Grasshopper getting information
==PAGE:24==
6
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
from Rhino, for detailed information regarding Revit and Dynamo see Section 2.4.
Examples of VP being used to drive more intelligent structures can be found in the
Autodesk classes mentioned earlier and (Shi and Yang, 2013), (K. M. Kensek, 2014),
(Rahmani, Zarrinmehr, et al., 2015), (Hejnfelt, 2016), (Konis, Gamas, and K. Kensek,
2016).
Figure 1.1: Opportunity for improving building design by improving knowledge and developing
supporting computational tools for the early design phase
A potential issue of attempting structural optimization in the early design phase is the
variance and complexity of buildings making an analysable model diﬃcult to obtain
(Shi and Yang, 2013). Companies having both architects and engineers in-house might
be able to avoid these diﬃculties by introducing company standards and best practice.
The separation of the engineers needing to implement early analysis, and the architects
performing design changes results in a project where no one can see the entire picture,
only their own corner.
The collaboration is thus essential, as a limited number of architects can or wish to write
optimization codes (Shi and Yang, 2013) making a collaboration with an engineering
partner preferable. Today however, the industry consists of a silo-separation with a
focus on sub-optimization rather than optimizing in the bigger picture.
By working more eﬃciently together, it is possible to bring a better, cheaper and more
buildable project to life for the client and the industry as a whole (Biilmann, 2015).
The current reluctance can stem from management, insuﬃcient abilities, the project
complexity or the subjective deﬁnition of the most optimal model as the optimal model
is a matter of perspective and compromises.
==PAGE:25==
1.5. INTERVIEWS
7
1.5
Interviews
This section describes the interviews conducted throughout the thesis. The interviews
concern the number of project changes during the early design phase and what kind of
changes are most common, in addition to the presence of subjective evaluation criteria.
The interview questions are used for identifying interest towards early optimization and
preliminary analysis and determine the current work-ﬂow and most common project
changes occurring in the professional industry.
The questions have been answered by:
- Structural Engineer and Fagprojektleder René Bjerg Nielsen from Sweco Denmark
- Structural Engineer and Group leader Thomas Bo Frederiksen from Sweco Denmark
- Structural Engineer Keita Sata from Nihon Sekkei Japan
- Building Engineer Vincent Olthuis from abt Consulting Engineers Netherlands
The interviews are performed with professionals from multiple countries and companies,
thus uncovering possible diﬀerences in work-ﬂow between these. This yields a better
picture of the possible diversities within the industry. The interview answers can be
found in Appendix D, a summary of the answers and conclusions drawn upon these
can be found in Section 2.3. The interview questions can be found below:
• How often do a major project (design) changes take place after the early design
phase, and what is the most common cause for these changes?
• On what grounds are designs evaluated and “rated” during the early design?
• How do you see the possibilities of using a semi-automated process of performing
preliminary analysis on early project proposals and thus open for the possibilities
for making more informed decisions at an early stage
• To what degree do the models used in the early design phase contain relevant
information regarding key structural components?
• How does the project change work-ﬂow look from your perspective from starting
a process of delivering a ﬁnished project, what are the most common changes
etc.?
==PAGE:26==
==PAGE:27==
CHAPTER 2
Background
Throughout this chapter, a description of the work-ﬂow enabled by BIM and VDC and
the implications of a possible work-ﬂow change is discussed in regard to the early design
phase. The work-ﬂow supports the proposed semi-automated optimization which is
made possible using Visual Programming (VP). This chapter will describe the answers
gained from interviews with industry professionals and the possibilities of automation
and optimization in the early design phase. A list of the tools used throughout this
thesis is described in this chapter. Finally, the design process which is followed in the
development of the VP graphs and Python scripts are listed giving an overview of the
subsequent chapters of the thesis.
2.1
Possibilities
Design optimization is an abbreviation of satisfying performance. The performance
being that of a building or a subsystem within, and to most eﬀectively satisfy several
potentially conﬂicting criteria of performance without deteriorating upon the rest.
The possibilities within early design optimization and use of genetic optimization al-
gorithms on building projects work to aid the designer in decision making in the early
phases of the design. A prediction made by (FRI, 2018) foresees that by the year 2030
30% of the working hours within the AEC industry can be automatized globally. This
automation will naturally make way for diﬀerent kinds of positions as it was seen with
the industrial revolution and the introduction of the assembly line.
Currently the analysis tools available aim towards precision over ﬂexibility and gener-
ation of design alternatives (Rolvink, Breider, and Coenders, 2010). This is caused by
bringing in the engineer only when the initial design phase has been concluded. There-
fore, tools aimed at the early design phase make use of estimations and simpliﬁcations
making them less accurate than the tools used later. The tools used at the early stage
is intended to give a quantitative knowledge of various structural elements.
The tools described here are not replacing the designers, the tools will still require an
underlying model on which to perform optimization to function.
"Beneﬁts over traditional methods include greatly reduced time per design option trailed...
Improvements in performance for complex problems and increased rigour in the design
process." (Evins et al., 2012)
9
==PAGE:28==
10
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND
As described by Evins et al., 2012 the generation of numerous design-options can sig-
niﬁcantly reduce the time needed for evaluation of each option, improving the general
performance of the building.
To implement this work-ﬂow, designers need to reach an agreement on how to advance
the design and the documentation hereof. By early involvement of other collaborators,
such as engineers, contractors, and clients it is possible to generate a contractual agree-
ment giving key decision makers direct access to all models available, even if they do
not wish to work actively within them (Holzer, 2011).
This collaboration might be hindered by a presumption from architects that constraints
will be introduced by engineers who only wish to analyze a limited number of models
(Holzer, 2011). The introduction of optimization which aims speciﬁcally towards eval-
uating a broad solution space can work towards changing this presumption. The gap
between architect and engineers can thus be bridged giving the architect the desired
freedom while allowing for engineering analysis in the early design phases. As discussed
in Section 2.2 the contractors and consultants are usually not included in the early de-
sign phase as per traditional project setups (Holzer, 2011). This traditional project
setup will have to be revised to work towards a more digitalized work-ﬂow allowing for
automated genetic optimization in the early design phases.
2.2
Work-ﬂow today
The work-ﬂow of structural optimization and analysis of various designs are today car-
ried out in two camps, one being the architect who leans towards the aesthetics and
the other being the engineer who leans towards stability and eﬃciency (L. L. Beghini,
A. Beghini, et al., 2014).
This subdivision creates a gap which can at worst result in a compromise or an archi-
tectural structure which the engineer is to make work.
At best it results in a structure that by coincident satisfy the requirements of the var-
ious disciplines involved.
A structural optimization from the perspective of the engineer is a natural part of
the design process. As the purpose of engineering design is generally to reduce the use
of natural resources and keeping costs at a minimum (L. L. Beghini, Carrion, et al.,
2014), (Stromberg et al., 2012) with considerations for safety, serviceability, economy
and aesthetics (Flager, 2009) where the latter can be challenging to quantify.
Often AEC professionals make design decisions in the early phases with little to no
knowledge of the design performance and possible alternatives (Flager, 2009). This
method of decision making can result in expensive project changes and ineﬃcient de-
signs downstream.
The sub-divided work-ﬂow present today and the lack of computational tools and anal-
ysis in the early design stages can be attributed to a ﬁnancial aspect. The industry
today wish only to implement automated work-ﬂows if a clear ﬁnancial beneﬁt can
be found for the company rather than the good of the project (Holzer, 2011). This
sub-division causes a halt in the implementation of analytical tools in the early design
==PAGE:29==
2.2. WORK-FLOW TODAY
11
phase for engineering companies not having in-house architecture departments or ar-
chitecture companies not having in-house engineering departments.
Today’s work-ﬂow thus require an early establishment of economic compensation and
clear agreements on services and aim for the project to successfully implement compu-
tational tools.
An increasing number of architects have started using parametric modelling for the
design phase as this allows for quick adaption and changes. These parametric models
need to be as versatile as possible to support as wide a range of changes as possible
(Davis and Peters, 2013). The models, however, are in most cases not used for engi-
neering analysis caused by a lack of appropriate tools or knowledge.
The models can, if used correctly, be used for facilitating a meaningful way of commu-
nication across disciplines allowing the idea of BIM to be introduced in the early design
phases and generate more integrated work throughout the project (J. Wang, 2017).
A wide range of tools for structural analysis is available to the engineers such as:
Autodesk’s Robot Structural Analysis, Ansys, Simula’s Abaqus, and Strusoft’s FEM-
Design. These tools in themselves require a non-parametric model to be either imported
or modelled directly in the software. This task require a signiﬁcant technical knowledge
from the user. The software, thus, do not integrate well directly with the architectural
branches.
VDC methods have enabled the introduction of engineering practices into the early
design phases allowing evaluation of design performance. This accelerates the possibil-
ities of multidisciplinary designs striving for a Pareto optimal design1 (Fischer, 2006).
This however enabled by VDC, is not yet successfully implemented in today’s work-
ﬂow caused by either reluctance from the industry, ineﬃcient tools or personnel, or
the inability of realizing the potentials of implementing early analysis (Eastman et al.,
2008, p.202).
The current work-ﬂow as can be seen in Figure 2.12 consists of a mostly one-directional
information transfer with clear lines between the conceptual- and detailed design. The
ﬁgure has been created as a result of Chapter 1, Section 2.2 and interview answers
found in Appendix D.
The key performance indicators (KPI) displayed in the bottom of Figure 2.1 and 2.2
visualizes the number of iterations possible, the cost for performing iterations/design
changes, the time required for these changes and thus the ﬂexibility of adopting changes
at the current stage.
By allowing more natural cooperation between branches, it is possible to get a fast
veriﬁcation of designs and allow for easier interoperability, increasing the ﬂexibility
and number of iterations performed in the detailed design phase.
This will subse-
quently lower the amount of time and money required for performing multiple design
evaluations as can be seen in Figure 2.23.
1Pareto Optimal described in Section 2.5
2Software and personnel can vary, the ones displayed are representational
3Software and personnel can vary, the ones displayed are representational
==PAGE:30==
12
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND
Figure 2.1: Current work-ﬂow allowing for a limited number of design evaluations
Figure 2.2: Proposed work-ﬂow implementing structural optimization in the early design phase
==PAGE:31==
2.3. SUMMARY OF INTERVIEW ANSWERS
13
2.3
Summary of interview answers
In this section the answers collected in the interviews will be summarized. The inter-
views can be found in full in Appendix D.
Design changes and reasons for these commonly occur during the early design phase
of the project. These changes can come from numerous directions such as wishes from
the client or contractor, economic projections of the current design, or architectural
changes in reﬁning the design. Other reasons might include insuﬃcient detail in the
early stages leading to the need for re-design. The early design phase is thus prone to
signiﬁcant changes due to a number of causes.
The evaluation and choice of diﬀerent designs are carried out by the architect, contrac-
tor, and engineer in the early phases by listing pros and cons for each design proposal
and thus identify the most promising design. The focus of the responsible parties may
vary from project to project leading to no speciﬁc performance indicators for the early
design stages.
Knowledge of the main structural components is essential to perform structural calcu-
lations in the early phases. This information is commonly present when the engineer
is involved in the early design phases.
Generally, an increasing interest has emerged in the industry in introducing semi-
automated preliminary analysis and optimization of early designs for assisting in de-
termination of the most promising path to pursue. The semi-automated analysis and
optimization could thus have signiﬁcant impacts on the early design decisions.
The importance of using this semi-automation for guidance and not decision making
is stressed as the most optimal solution from an analysis is most times not the actual
optimal solution for all involved parties.
Changes before the conceptual design stage are inﬂuencing the work-ﬂow in the early
design stages. From the conceptual design to the project proposal the design is thor-
oughly analysed. The results of the analysis can bring design changes which at this
stage are costly to implement. The changes can stem from several directions such as
the client, architect or contractor.

CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND
2.4.1
Autodesk Revit
Autodesk Revit is a modelling software that "let you use the intelligent model-based
process to plan, design, construct, and manage buildings and infrastructure. Revit sup-
ports a multidiscipline design process for collaborative design." (Autodesk, 2018). Revit
is considered to be a BIM tool as it can hold extensive amounts of information and
relate these to element placement and orientation. The functionality of Revit allows
models from numerous ﬁelds to be linked into a shared model permitting collision con-
trol through add-ons and thus support BIM as Revit contains all drawing materials.
Revit is commonly used to perform tasks such as quantity estimations, collision control
and 3D modelling.
When a model is made in Revit the generation of an analytical model happens auto-
matically. The analytical model contains the default wall, ﬂoor, beams, braces, and
columns. A known challenge has been observed by Hejnfelt, 2016 ensuring an ana-
lytical model where nodes are connected and coupled together to allow correct load
transfer through the structure. For this thesis, it is assumed that an analytical model
that is connected correctly is present.
Autodesk Revit 2018.2 is used throughout this thesis.
2.4.2
Autodesk Robot Structural Analysis
Autodesk Robot Structural Analysis Professional (RSA) is a FEM software owned by
the Autodesk co-operation. This shared ownership of both Dynamo, Revit and RSA
eases the possible interaction between the various software.
RSA and the RSA Application Programming Interface (API) is used as a primary tool
for structural optimization and automation with the functionality available in RSA.
The RSA API uses Microsoft’s component object model (COM) technology which
makes it possible to create a .NET application for controlling the RSA analysis (Fisher
and Sharma, 2010).
Autodesk Robot Structural Analysis Professional 2018 is used throughout this thesis.
2.4.3
Dynamo
Dynamo is a Visual Programming tool which comes in two versions, Dynamo and Dy-
namo Studio. Dynamo Studio works as a standalone application without the link to
the Revit API; this version will not be used in this thesis. Dynamo was launched in
2012 as an add-in for BIM on top of the Revit API and the .NET framework (Tammik,
2005) and now comes as an integrated part of Revit 2018, Dynamo has a DesignScript
functionality that Revit does not.
Version 2.0 was released in April 2018 and bugs are still being discovered including
severe bugs in the interaction with RSA through the "Structural Analysis for Dynamo"
package the analysis carried out in this thesis is thus performed in version 1.3.2. Dy-
namos look and functionality is inﬂuenced by applications such as MaxMSP, Maya
Hypergraph, Lego Mindstorms Nxt, Bentleys Generative Components and Grasshop-
per (K. M. Kensek, 2014). Since the launch, the number of custom packages has grown
vastly and currently stands at 1328 packages from 513 authors, while Dynamo has 1.2
==PAGE:33==
2.4. TOOLS
15
million installs. Likewise, the Dynamo Forum works to share knowledge and experience
about Dynamo and further the number of useful packages available.
The diﬀerence between Visual Programming and standard textual programming is
the connection of bits of code compared to writing the code. An example of the two
can be seen in Figure 2.3 both resulting in the circle seen to the right.
Figure 2.3: Example of visual- and textual programming

and mapping of structures.
For a general understanding of Dynamo and VP, one can refer to the open source
project, Dynamo Primer (Autodesk, 2017).

in Dynamo. The advantage of this is the ability to parameterize a structure; once a

example of a parametric structure modelled in Dynamo is shown in Figure 2.4. The
structure is modelled so the user can use sliders to easily control height, width, length
and number of cross-braces.

Custom Nodes
Dynamo oﬀers the possibility of building custom nodes, the custom node is a container
of nodes and can be recognized by its yellow background. The custom nodes can be
used for reducing the perceived complexity of the graph to the user, to share with the
community, or to be more adaptive to changes. A custom node can be seen in Figure
2.7.
Figure 2.7: A custom node in Dynamo
The custom nodes allow the user to have several copies of a process and by conducting
a change in one place inside the custom node, the change will be replicated in all
instances of the custom node.
There are several ways of building custom nodes, for programmers, it is possible to
develop zero-touch nodes that are created using C# and are locked to outside changes.
==PAGE:36==
18
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND
For non-programmers, it is possible to work directly with the Dynamo UI and create
custom nodes from clusters of nodes deﬁning multiple inputs and outputs (Autodesk,
2017).
In this thesis, the custom nodes are used in connection with the package Optimo for
specifying inputs in an optimization algorithmic loop.
2.4.4
Optimo
Optimo is a package for Dynamo developed by Rahmani, Stoupine, et al., 2015. Op-
timo is an open-source VP-based Multi-Objective Optimization (MOO) for BIM-based
operations through the interaction with Dynamo. The package was published in 2015
as an open-source addition to the Dynamo library. Optimo works based on a modiﬁed
version of the Nondominated Sorting Genetic Algorithm-II (NSGA-II). The algorithm
works by generating a set of random numbers in a range and uses these as parents for
the optimization algorithm. On each iteration the optimal children created are used
as parents working towards the most optimal solution as long as the stop criterion has
not yet been reached as can be seen in Figure 2.13. Further information regarding the
algorithm can be found in Section 2.5.
Optimo works based on the .NET version of the algorithms incorporated in the Java
version called jMetal. Generally, the Optimo setup can be described in ﬁve steps as
can be seen in Figure 2.8.
Figure 2.8: Optimo general setup (Rahmani, Stoupine, et al., 2015)
User Inputs
The user inputs contain the population size, number of objectives and variables. The
population size is the number of random values created between the upper and lower
limits, the larger the population size, the better the result. The precision of the opti-
mization is related to the added computation time required for larger precision. Ac-
cording to (Rahmani, Stoupine, et al., 2015) there is no limit to the population size and
tests have been run with up to N=5000. The number of objectives deﬁnes the number
of ﬁtness functions that are to be included in the optimization. The objectives would
each contain a custom node and a ﬁtness function. The variables are the range of so-
lutions from lower to upper bounds or contain a list of variables. The ﬁtness functions
list is a list that contains the ﬁtness functions created as custom nodes in Dynamo. The
==PAGE:37==
2.4. TOOLS
19
ﬁtness is a single value that represents the general ﬁtness of the solution this allows
the user to use external functions without the need to change the optimization source
code which would require programming expertise.
Initial Population List
The initial population is a list of values in between the upper and lower limits. These
initial values are used to randomly generate the ﬁrst population of solutions which
assigns the ﬁtness values to the population list.
Generation and Sorting Loop
This is the central part of Optimo, the generation and sorting loop iterates until it
reaches the count of iterations set by the user. The initial population list parents are
crossed with the ﬁtness score and the optimal options children, are chosen as the new
parents through the nondominant sorting algorithm.
Export of results
The export of the optimal result happens in regards to the Pareto front, see Section
2.5. They can be exported to ".csv" or written directly to a BIM model.
The setup of Optimo in Dynamo can be seen in Figure 2.9 displaying the user in-
put as the "population and objective" and "custom node", the Function.Apply node
works by taking the ﬁtness function and applying this to the initial solution list. This
list is combined with the decision variables in the AssignFitnessFuncResults node in-
side the Function node. The optimal solutions are assigned and sorted generating an
oﬀspring population. This is used in the following iteration. The sorting takes place
inside the Function node through the NondominatedSorting node which ensures elitism,
and ﬁnally the possibility for export of the results.
The following papers have worked on validating and testing Optimo (Rahmani, Stoupine,
et al., 2015), (Rahmani, Bergin, et al., 2014), (Vermeulen, 2017a) and (Rahmani,
Zarrinmehr, et al., 2015).
Figure 2.9: Example of a generic Optimo setup
Optimo version 0.1.2 is used throughout this thesis.

2.4.6
Python
Python works as a multi-paradigm, object-oriented, programming language used for
many general purposes within a variety of ﬁelds including, but not limited to, mathe-
matics, data-science, and web-development.
The Python integration used in Dynamo works through IronPython which is an imple-
mentation of Python created by Microsoft in C# and enables Python to communicate
with the .NET framework (Talarico, 2017).
Python is a way of overcoming limitations of VP such as the limited possibility of per-
forming loops. Python includes the option to access the Revit-, and RSA-API directly
which cannot be reached through Dynamo by conventional methods. As the API can
be complicated to navigate using both the Dynamo community and Autodesk support
becomes necessary as no documentation for either the Revit API or RSA API exists
for Python programming.
An example of a custom script using the RSA API is the creation of custom re-
leases. This enables the use of any release in RSA and can be automatically created
and changed by the user without direct interaction with the current RSA session; an
overview of developed Python scripts for this thesis can be found in Table 2.3.
In Figure 2.10 the creation of the same structure as in Section 2.4.3 is created using
a Python node in Dynamo showing a much smaller VP graph as most processes are
handled inside Python.

2.4.7
Limitations
Dynamo
As Dynamo is a surrogate program to Revit, as described in Section 2.4.3, the hard-
ware requirements are substantial. It has been found that up to 3GB of RAM being
used executing graphs along with a memory leak. If a graph runs for a long time, it
will build up the amount of RAM used as also observed by Pavlov, 2015. This will
have a minor impact on the graphs and the development time as there is a limit to the
hardware available to the Author.
Optimo
An issue between Dynamo, the package Optimo, and RSA has been observed. The
complexity of the run is limited by the amount of RAM available. The following cor-
relation has been observed: 8GB RAM ≈50 runs while 16GB RAM ≈100 runs, the
correlation is veriﬁed by Vincent Olthuis from abt Consulting Engineers. The only
solution to this is to save the current population and restart RSA, Dynamo, and Revit
and use the saved population as initial population. This increases the development and
execution time. It is hoped that by version 2.0 of Dynamo changing from XML format
to the JSON format, a more stable build will be reached and thus solve the issue with
better memory handling. Tests have been carried out using Dynamo 2.0 discovering
the need for an update of the structural analysis package to support this version prior
to the conversion of the work performed in this thesis from v. 1.3.2 to 2.0.
==PAGE:40==
22
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND
RSA
The pre-developed RSA nodes released by Autodesk are limited in the interaction with
RSA. This reduces the possible complexity of structural models available. In this thesis
the Author have proposed minor workarounds using Python coding and direct interac-
tion with the RSA API. However, currently, no documentation for the use of Python
and the RSA API exists making the scripting very time-consuming as it is performed
by trial and error.
The RSA/Dynamo interaction is timing sensitive. As the structure is being created and
calculated in RSA directly through Dynamo the timing between the diﬀerent actions
are essential as it is not possible to set supports that have not yet been created or add
loads to a bar that does not exist. A solution to this is proposed in this thesis.
Execution time
The time required for execution of an optimization graph depends on the complexity of
the structure. Complex structures take between 5-10 minutes to generate in RSA. By
combining the work-ﬂow into a custom node this time can be reduced to 1-3 minutes.
With the access to more processing power, this time can be further reduced. For devel-
opment purposes, the possibility of freezing parts of the graph in Dynamo is utilized.
2.5
Optimization types and methodologies
Genetic Optimization
Genetic optimization is a technique that makes use of Genetic Algorithms (GA). GA’s
initially take oﬀwith an initial solution list, randomly generated within a speciﬁc range.
For each of these values, an operation which deﬁnes the optimization problem is eval-
uated, and a single ﬁtness value is returned corresponding with the weighted rating
of the solution. The inspiration for the GA comes from natural selection where the
initial population is evaluated and crossed with each other producing oﬀspring. Each
iteration can be seen as a generation, where the oﬀspring originates from the parent’s
pool of information with the possibility of random mutations. Usually, the algorithm
terminates when the number of iterations has been reached, or a speciﬁc predeﬁned
ﬁtness value is found (Eiben, Raué, and Ruttkay, 1994).
GA’s works as a Multi-Objective Optimization (MOO) which require a single ﬁtness
measure that incorporate all evaluation criteria included in the optimization. Opposed
to Single-Objective Optimization (SOO) where the function itself can be used for eval-
uation. The general optimization work-ﬂow can be seen in Figure 2.11.
Figure 2.11: General optimization work-ﬂow
==PAGE:41==
2.5. OPTIMIZATION TYPES AND METHODOLOGIES
23
Pareto Optimization
Optimizing using Pareto optimization developed by Vilfredo Pareto (Kanbur, 2005).
The solutions take form after a Pareto front which is a boundary created in the so-
lution space displaying all the possible designs.
The boundary represents a set of
solutions which are non-dominated by any other solutions. A non-dominated solution
is a solution where no alternative designs exist that can be improved without deteri-
orating at least one other objective. An illustration of the Pareto front to an MOO
problem can be seen in Figure 2.12 showing two objectives. If three or more objectives
are present, it becomes diﬃcult to visualize the Pareto front accurately.
Figure 2.12: Pareto front illustrated (Klammer et al., 2015)
NSGA-II
NSGA-II is short for Natural Selection Genetic Algorithm. NSGA-II is an evolutionary,
elitist, multi-objective, non-dominated, genetic optimization algorithm. As one of the
most popular MOO algorithms (Deb et al., 2002) the NSGA-II delivers a fast non-
dominated sorting approach, fast crowded distance estimation procedure and a simple
crowded comparison operator (Yusoﬀ, Ngadiman, and Zain, 2011).
The NSGA-II
consists of 6 steps as can be seen in Figure 2.13. By the use of elitism, the NSGA-II
does not allow for a Pareto optimal solution to be deleted while maintaining a maximum
complexity of O(MN 2) where M=number of objective functions, N=Population size,
and O=Upper bound (Eiben, Raué, and Ruttkay, 1994).
==PAGE:42==
24
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND
Figure 2.13: NSGA-II algorithm
NSGA-II can be expressed as seen in Table 2.4 as NSGA-II follows the Genetic Evo-
lutionary algorithmic form. The advantages of the NSGA-II algorithm is the explicit
diversity preservation, the limited complexity, and the elitism.
The disadvantages
are the possibility of a crowded comparison that restricts the convergence and a non-
dominated sorting with the size of 2N.
t ←−0
Generation Counter
C0
Initial Population
while stopping condition(s) not true do
Create new generation
Evaluate ﬁtness, f(x), of each individual x ∈Ct
Perform reproduction to create offspring
Select the new population Ct+1
t ←−t + 1
Advance to new generation
end while
Table 2.4: Genetic evolution algorithm (Curry and Dagli, 2014)
2.6
Design Process
This section describes the proposed design process developed throughout this thesis.
The goal is a semi-automated structural optimization design process.
The process
should not limit the architectural freedom but aid the user in developing a more in-
telligent design by using VP, GAs’ and structural analysis complimenting the design
process.
The developed graphs are intended for use in the early design phase.
The graphs
are tested on three diﬀerent cases verifying the functionality. The graphs serve as a
preliminary analysis using a FEM tool for calculation and semi-automatic evaluation,
ranking, and visualization of the proposed designs utilizing genetic algorithms.
==PAGE:43==
2.6. DESIGN PROCESS
25
A challenge is to generate adaptable models from static ones, utilizing data not in-
tended for changes. By doing so setting up a work-ﬂow allowing for repeated changes
to non-parametric BIM models while maintaining continuity. Table 2.5 shows the de-
sign process developed for structural optimization of steel structures in the early design
phase developed throughout this thesis.
Table 2.5: Design Process
0. The user sets limits and stop criterion
User-deﬁned optimization run is deﬁned in 2 steps
0.1 The user sets upper and lower limits
Limits are deﬁned by the user for design space
0.2 Population and Generation size is set by the user
Number of iterations controlled by Population and Generation is set
1. Prepare model
The non-parametric model is investigated and modiﬁed in two steps
1.1 Extract model geometry and properties from non-parametric model
The geometry and properties from the non-parametric model are extracted
using Python programming
1.2 Modify extracted geometry into parametric geometry
The extracted geometry is made parametric using Visual Programming
2. Generate FEM model
Generate a complete FEM model in 4 steps
2.1 Generate analytical bars
Create analytical bars of mutated geometry
2.2 Assign boundary conditions
Assign boundary conditions to model
2.3 Assign sections, releases, and materials
Assign properties to model
2.4 Create load-cases and specify loads
Generate load-cases and loads for the model
3. Calculate model and extract results
Initiate calculation and prepare the desired results
4. Rank design and return to step 2 if the stop criterion is not met
Rank, and record design option and restart process
4.1 Evaluate results and rank design
Evaluate and rank the design using Python programming
4.2 Record evaluated design and save data to .csv
Record evaluation and rank of design using Python programming
4.3 Restart process if design criterion is not met
Start from step 2 if the stop criterion is not met
5. Visualize all results in a parallel-coordinates plot
Visualize a combination of all results from optimization using Python
programming for generating an interactive parallel-coordinates plot
Throughout the thesis, the diﬀerent steps will be explained and illustrated in 3 diﬀerent
cases of a 2D truss, 3D complex structure and a steel frame building.
The aim is to generate Dynamo work-ﬂows allowing the user to perform diﬀerent types
==PAGE:44==
26
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND
of structural optimization. It should be noted that the process is transparent allowing
the user to see all steps and make changes to the graphs/scripts in the semi-automated
work-ﬂows. It would be possible to perform the analysis and ratings without the use
of programming or VP. This would require large amounts of manual work and time.
However, manual optimization is possible but not practical.
Both Python- and visual-programming are essential tools in the design process. These
tools work to semi-automate the work and improve the design speed. This allows for
more iterations being evaluated and analysed working towards more intelligent design.
The proposed process can also be used for MOO between diﬀerent ﬁelds of engineering
this is however not investigated in this thesis.
The material available for performing structural optimization using Dynamo and RSA
is insuﬃcient. This is shown by the limitations in packages available along with dis-
cussions on the Dynamo forum. The interaction with RSA through Dynamo is not yet
widely used caused by the limited number of nodes available and the steep learning
curve for writing directly to the API. The RSA API is likewise only sparingly used along
with Python programming; the tasks carried out in this thesis displays a number of
possibilities using the API for interacting with RSA through Python. This means that
the design process proposed requires the use of custom scripting, all scripts developed
for the thesis can be found in Appendix C.
2.7
Sub-conclusion
The current work-ﬂow and the limitations of this have been presented along with a
proposal for a work-ﬂow which include sub-optimization in the early phases.
The impact and possibilities of the implementation have been discussed. From this, it
can be concluded that there is a basis for developing a semi-automatic design process
using structural optimization at an early stage ﬁtting into the mindset of BIM and
VDC.
The work-ﬂow should take advantage of the FEM analysis software for performing
structural analysis and VP for performing design optimization, decisions, and itera-
tions.
The tools to be used for the development of the work-ﬂow is presented, and the handling
of Python scripts and Dynamo graphs are explained along with limitations related to
each tool.
Finally, the genetic optimization algorithms used in the development process have been
explained, showing the evolutionary nature of these.
==PAGE:45==
CHAPTER 3
Optimization of steel truss
This chapter focus on the optimization of a steel truss provided by Sweco; the truss
can be seen in Figure 3.1. By looking into optimizing upon the sections used in the
truss as well as the total height of the truss an automatic optimization is deemed pos-
sible. This optimization process will work towards minimizing the material usage and
physical size of the truss. By granting the user the option to choose from a list of
sections, the maximum and minimum height of the truss and the number of diﬀerent
sections to be used. The last point works towards a more buildable project minimizing
the complexity of the ﬁnal product by favoring solutions using fewer section types.
To successfully rate the outcomes of the analysis, evaluation factors need to be de-
termined to rank the generated structure according to the methodology of MOO as
described in Section 2.5.
The truss spans 17 meters and is to be used in a theater building, giving rise to a
need for a truss that is low, to not obstruct the view of the users. The model is from
the early design phase where decisions regarding the truss type had yet to be made.
The choice between an N- or K-truss respectively is thus oﬀered along with section
types and total truss height.
Sweco delivers the truss in an RSA ﬁle format as the modelling had yet to occur in
Revit when the decision regarding truss layout had to be made. The truss will serve
as an example and veriﬁcation on which to run the graphs and scripts developed.
Two versions of the graphs have been developed illustrating the two methods of mod-
elling that is most commonly used in the early design phase. One is controlled using
geometry captured from RSA which can be used on existing and ongoing projects. An-
other is created entirely parametric within Dynamo giving the option of quick changes
illustrating the models coming from larger architectural companies.
Figure 3.1: Truss delivered by Sweco as an RSA model.
27
==PAGE:46==
28
CHAPTER 3. OPTIMIZATION OF STEEL TRUSS
The developed work-ﬂow using Dynamo graphs is presented and explained throughout
this chapter. The ranking of solutions and recording of data will happen through a
Python script which records the results and relevant data and writes these to a .csv
ﬁle for future analysis. An overview of all graphs and scripts in this chapter can be
found in Table 3.1.

With the use of the developed graphs and scripts shown in Table 3.1, the structure is
optimized according to user input and desire. The graphs and scripts are explained
throughout this chapter.
3.1
Prepare model
To prepare the model for structural analysis in RSA, the geometry to be analysed need
to be identiﬁed and grouped by the desired optimization parameters. The parameters
chosen in this case are sections, truss height, and truss type. These parameters could,
however, be anything: Number of spans, steel-type, length, width, releases, connection-
types, etc.
==PAGE:47==
3.1. PREPARE MODEL
29
3.1.1
Model geometry extraction from RSA
There are several ways that the designer can deliver information about a model. There-
fore, two diﬀerent graphs have been developed for the geometric model creation. This
serves to show two ways of approaching the challenge and oﬀer the designer the tools
to explore solutions and alternatives without restrictions.
The model delivered by Sweco consist of an RSA model. A script is developed for
extracting the geometry from the model automatically, writing these to a .csv ﬁle for
the future recreation of the model for optimization purposes. The script developed for
this can be seen in Figure 3.2.
The option for the designer to deliver the model in an entirely parametric model stands.
This could be done as shown in the graph in Figure 3.3 where a fully parametric truss
is created in Dynamo allowing for rapid change of height, width, truss type, and par-
titions.
Figure 3.2: The extraction of data from RSA using Python. Script 1.1 in Table 3.1
Figure 3.2 shows the Python script created for extracting geometrical and load data
from active RSA session. The script can be found in text format in Appendix C, point
by point explanation of the script is shown in Table 3.2.
Table 3.2: Script explanation - Extract information from RSA
1. Connect to RSA API
2. Import RSA objects
3. Prepare empty lists and output format for future information
4. Capture all information regarding bars, cases, nodes, and labels
5. Get information of bars using "for" loop
6. Get information of nodes using "for" loop; nodes found using step 5 ignored
7. Get information regarding loads and load-cases using nested "for" loop
8. Return all processed information in pre-prepared lists from step 3
9. Information returned is written to either Excel or .csv as decided by the user.
==PAGE:48==
30
CHAPTER 3. OPTIMIZATION OF STEEL TRUSS
The script in Figure 3.2 connects to the active RSA-session and -API through the
.dll ﬁle interop.RobotOM.dll. The ﬁle is located in the OS system AppData folder in
the Dynamo package Structural Analysis for Dynamo and inside the installation of
RSA. By connecting directly to the RSA API and researching the appropriate calls for
extraction of data a connection can be created to the active RSA-session.
With the use of for-loops, all start- and end-nodes of bars in the active session is
captured and written to a list along with the bar number. This list is then compared
to the nodes found and any nodes which are not start- or end-nodes are saved separately.
Finally, a nested loop is performed evaluating all load cases one at a time iterating over
the number of loads applied in each case. All information that was prepared as lists in
Table 3.2, step 3 is returned as the output of the Python script.
3.1.2
Fully geometric model using VP
Alternatively, a fully parametric model can be created.
This removes the need for
pulling data from a predeﬁned model. As an increasing number of designers create
models using parametric tools and VP environments (Negendahl, 2015) the possibility
of obtaining a parametric model is increasing. This way of modelling is commonly used
in the early design phase as it is more ﬂexible to changes. An example of a parametric
model of the truss in Dynamo can be seen in Figure 3.3. The truss is created with
sliders deﬁning the height and width, an integer deﬁning the number of divisions and
an option to choose between a K- and N-truss types by writing K or N as a string
input.
Figure 3.3: Creation of parametric truss. Graph 1.2 in Table 3.1
Figure 3.3 shows the Dynamo graph (left) and examples of K- and N-trusses (right)
created for modelling a geometric truss. Point by point explanation of the graph can
be found in Table 3.3.
Table 3.3: Graph explanation - Creation of parametric truss using VP
1. Specify input
2. Sort and organize input
3. Create bars/lines from sorted data
4. Python script controlling truss type
Using number sliders, integer inputs and a simple Python script the creation of the
truss is possible and ﬂexible in both height, width, number of braces, truss type (K
or N), steel type, releases, and sections. With these inputs being easily accessible the
==PAGE:49==
3.2. RSA ANALYTICAL MODEL
31
creation of a truss takes seconds, and the user is presented with instant visual feedback
of the truss. The graph generates the truss using the inputs chosen by the user.
3.1.3
Create and organize members
The creation and organization of the members are performed by the captured start-
and endpoints of bars and nodes from RSA as seen in Figure 3.2. The graph is simple
as its only purpose is to read the data from Excel or ".csv" format organizing the
data by grouping it in lists of bars with similar properties.
This listing allows for
the possibility to later assign varying sections, materials, and releases to the bars in
addition to changing the height and truss type.
Figure 3.4: Creation of geometry from extracted geometry. Graph 2 in Table 3.1
Figure 3.4 shows the Dynamo graph created for generating a truss from extracted
information from RSA. Point by point explanation of the graph can be found in Table
3.4.
Table 3.4: Graph explanation - Creation of geometry using extracted values from Section
3.1.1
1. Import geometry from ".csv" or Excel
2. Sort and organize input
3. Create bars/lines from sorted data
4. Python script controlling truss type
The data from .csv ﬁles are imported to Dynamo using a custom Python script which
can be found in Appendix C. The Python script returns strings as strings, numbers
as numbers and integers as integers opposed to the build in the .csv reader which
returns all information from .csv as strings. This conversion is essential as the Dynamo
environment cannot generate geometry from string elements.
The information is sorted and grouped in relation to connections and placements and
prepared for the creation of an analytical model in RSA.
3.2
RSA analytical model
Autodesk has released a number of nodes that work to create a link between Dynamo
and RSA, the content of the package Structural Analysis for Dynamo contain these
nodes. The nodes can handle most common tasks and object creation in RSA from
==PAGE:50==
32
CHAPTER 3. OPTIMIZATION OF STEEL TRUSS
Dynamo. If more advanced tasks are required the need for writing directly to the RSA
API arise. As this project scope towards the early design phase, most of the processes
are included in the pre-developed nodes. If speciﬁc needs arise, an investigation into
the RSA API is performed and described throughout this thesis.
3.2.1
Set sections, releases, materials, supports and loads
Figure 3.5: Creation of the boundary conditions and RSA analytical model from organized members.

The graph is controlled by the user using the inputs for load values, section types, loads,

values speciﬁed by the user. As an interaction between applications is necessary, it
should be expected that the model creation takes a few minutes depending on model
complexity.

==PAGE:52==
34
CHAPTER 3. OPTIMIZATION OF STEEL TRUSS
note that a timing issue between Dynamo and RSA is present, as described in Section
2.4.7, and a node delaying the assignment of the newly created releases or supports
should be used.
The delay is accomplished using the command "{waitfor,pass}[1]"
inside a code block. This waits for the input from "waitfor" before it passes the "pass"
value. The dummy output from the Python script is used as the "waitfor" condition
ensuring the successful creation of the supports and releases before assignment of these.
3.3
Perform calculation and retrieve results
The calculation of the structure is possible when it has been created in RSA, and
the information described in Section 3.2.1 is present. The calculation step is carried
out using the build in the engine of RSA. By specifying which results are needed
in Dynamo, these can be pulled from the calculation. To calculate the project the
following information needs to be passed to the Analysis.Calculate node: Analytical
bars (and shells), supporting nodes, load cases, and superimposed load deﬁnitions.
3.3.1
Retrieving and analysing results
Using the output of the Analysis.Calculate node it is possible to request results. De-
pending on which results are needed varying inputs are required with the one universal
need being a "loadcase" input specifying an integer value representing a load case, to
capture this the LoadCase.ID node is used.
Figure 3.7: Obtain results and perform unit conversions. Graph 4.1 in Table 3.1
Figure 3.7 shows the Dynamo graph used for retrieving results from calculation. Point
by point explanation of the graph can be found in Table 3.8.
Table 3.8: Graph explanation - Dynamo graph used for retrieving the desired results from the
active RSA project
1. Perform calculation. The possibility of automatically saving the model is offered to the user
2. Get max stresses and transform from
N
mm2 to N
m2
3. Take absolute value as no differentiation between tension and compression is made
4. Get reactions from self-weight and convert to kg
5. Pass to Python script for evaluation
Using the "Analysis.Calculate" or the "Analysis.CalculateWithSave" node it is possible
to instantiate the calculation in RSA. Subsequently, the results are made available and
==PAGE:53==
3.3. PERFORM CALCULATION AND RETRIEVE RESULTS
35
can be extracted using, e.g. the "Barstresses.GetMaxValuesList" node. It should be
noted that Dynamo receives the results in SI units from RSA as the default unit set
inside RSA of the Author is SI. The self-weight of the structure is calculated automat-
ically taking the index of the load case containing only self-weight and using this for
retrieving reaction forces. Finally, the information is passed to the Python script which
evaluate and assign a rating to the current design.
Figure 3.8: Using Python for evaluating and ranking the results. Script 4.2 in Table 3.1
Figure 3.8 shows the Python script which evaluates the results and appends these to
a ".csv" ﬁle. The script can be found in text format in Appendix C. Point by point
explanation of the script can be found in Table 3.9.
Table 3.9: Python script used for evaluating, rating and recording the results pulled from RSA
1. Create deﬁnition for appending information to .csv ﬁle
2. Specify input from Dynamo can be seen in Table 3.10
3. Iterate through all bars and evaluate in regards to max stress set by the user
4. Record the weight-score for the current model
5. Write the ﬁnal result to the .csv ﬁle with information which can be found in Table 3.10
==PAGE:54==
36
CHAPTER 3. OPTIMIZATION OF STEEL TRUSS
Evaluating and assigning a weight-score to the design option is essential for rating the
various designs according to the method of MOO as described in Section 2.5. This is
performed by retrieving the overall self-weight of the structure and for each member
that is either over- or under-stressed a penalty weight is added. As no over-stressed
members are allowed the penalty for over-stressed members is signiﬁcantly higher than
that of under-stressed members. The solution with the rating closest to the self-weight
is rated as the most optimal solution. The Python script performs this action using
a for-loop iterating over each bar. Finally, the results are appended to a .csv ﬁle for
further analysis.

IN[3] = Self weight
Stress value
IN[4] = Max allowable stress
Truss type
IN[5] = Penalty weight min
Self-weight
IN[6] = Penalty weight max
Top section index
IN[7] = Min ratio

IN[8] = Max ratio

3.4. OPTIMIZATION USING OPTIMO

3.4
Optimization using Optimo
As described in Section 2.4.4 the Optimo package requires the creation of custom nodes.
These custom nodes are a collection of nodes which are grouped and saved locally in
the ".dyf" format. These nodes can be shared online through the package manager
to the Dynamo community. The custom nodes created for this optimization work by
collecting the previously described nodes nesting these in a single node with variable
inputs and output as described in Section 2.4.3.
3.4.1
Core node
The core node as shown in Figure 3.10 require multiple inputs and works as an ordinary
node in dynamo reducing the size of the graph signiﬁcantly. By allowing the user to
insert the parameters which are static and leaving the options which are changeable,
multiple options can be evaluated for the core node.
Figure 3.10: Core node for reading an excel/.csv ﬁle (left) and geometric truss (right)
Figure 3.11: Combining graphs and for variable input and output. Graph 5.1 in Table 3.1
Figure 3.11 shows the content of the custom node seen in Figure 3.11 as a Dynamo
graph. Point by point explanation of the graph can be found in Table 3.11.
==PAGE:56==
38
CHAPTER 3. OPTIMIZATION OF STEEL TRUSS
Table 3.11: Graph explanation - Core node for optimization of structural truss
1. Inputs as shown on custom nodes specifying input data type and name
2. Create custom support deﬁnition
3. Read from Excel or .csv
4. Create geometry from information
5. Set boundary conditions in RSA and create loads
6. Calculate project and sort results
7. Evaluate results in Python script returning a single weight-score
8. Output the weight-score to the core node
It is possible to add and remove the inputs and outputs of the core node. By adding/re-
moving options the user will have to interact with Dynamo, and basic knowledge is
required for performing changes.
3.4.2
Fitness node
The ﬁtness node works by leaving the parameters to be optimized empty. This gives
Dynamo the possibility to populate these with data. In the case of genetic optimization
these will consist of the population. The information from the current population is
combined with the weight-score returned by the core node and an output function
including the combined values is returned by the node outputting the ﬁtness of the
current population.
Figure 3.12: Fitness node for reading an excel/.csv ﬁle (left) and geometric truss (right)

in the analysis.

By exporting the population from the "loop-while" node to a .csv ﬁle, it is possible upon
restart of Revit and RSA to reinitialize the process. This allows compensating for the
current RAM issues discussed in Section 2.4.7. To do this successfully, it is necessary
to add a code block indicating that the pass of information from the "loop-while" node
to the write to .csv node should be delayed until the current run has ﬁnished. This is
done with the command "{waitfor,pass}[1]" inside a code block.
==PAGE:58==
40
CHAPTER 3. OPTIMIZATION OF STEEL TRUSS
3.5
Results
For visualization of the results a Python script automatically generates a parallel coor-
dinates plot for the user. This presents the user with all the results of the analysis in
a comprehensive and interactive way. The structural models in RSA for each design
alternative tested are likewise available for comprehensive and further manual analysis.
The library of models can be used for verifying results and helps to avoid the need for
future remodelling.
By using an interactive interface in a .html format, the results are made readily avail-
able to the user and scoping in on a solution is made more natural than the current
tabular form used by the industry (Joyce, 2015).
For visualization of the analysis and the convergence towards an optimal solution in
regards to the design criteria set for the analysis, a scatter plot is created in Figure
3.15. This displays the weight-score along the y-axis and the iteration count along the
x-axis. The convergence is seen as a result of the use of genetic algorithms working
towards a more optimal solution.
Figure 3.15: Results of analysis in scatter plot
The plots seen in Figure 3.16 and 3.17 have been created using Python and the packages
Plotly and Pandas. The script is used for automatic creation of the interactive plots
from the extracted ".csv" data recorded from the optimization runs. These parallel
coordinates plots can be used as assistance in discovering desirable results and get a
quick and interactive overview to show clients and coworkers. Using this can indicate
the possible implications of choosing one option over another and assist in later decision
making. The current plot holds more than 345.000 points of data.
The script has to be customized for the data being recorded in the .csv ﬁle by the
optimization run; this is a one-time operation. The script for generating the plot can
be found in Appendix C.
==PAGE:59==
3.5. RESULTS
41
Figure 3.16: Results in interactive parallel coordinates graph from ﬁrstly generated solution (blue) to
lastly generated solution (red) showing convergence towards the lowest possible weight-score
By using the cursor, the user can interact with the plots in a ".html" site. This site can
either be hosted for the client or sent to external personnel. With this comes the ability
to highlight speciﬁc parts of the plot and display the chosen path(s) as illustrated in
Figure 3.17. A sample of .html plots can be found in the digital Annex, large versions
of the illustrations can be found in Appendix F.
Figure 3.17: A single path from Figure 3.16 highlighted by applying a ﬁlter using the cursor automati-
cally displaying the chosen path
A particular interest for the original design of the truss was keeping the deﬂection at a
reasonable level. The reason being that the truss is carrying the roof and upper levels
above a theater room and deﬂections might obscure vision for the guests and seem
unsafe. By reducing the overall height of the truss, a better experience is achieved for
guests in addition to a slightly larger deﬂection allowance than the original truss.
