The automatic creation of solution alternatives and the visualization graph work to-
gether towards a better understanding of possible designs. The use of a genetic algo-
rithm makes it possible to faster scope towards optimal solutions and thus save time
==PAGE:62==
44
CHAPTER 3. OPTIMIZATION OF STEEL TRUSS
in the design phase, allowing the exploration of a larger solution space.
The model generation and evaluation time for the truss is approximately 20 seconds.
The generation and calculation time can be compared to the time needed by a designer
in changing parameters at random searching for a more optimal solution. This can
be both tiresome, repetitive and time-consuming and the number of iterations using a
manual method is limited.
Having a stronger computer, or server, set up for the model generation and calculation
the time required could be decreased, or the number of models evaluated could be
increased.
Veriﬁcation of a single truss have been performed displaying an average deviation of
2.7MPa from the automatically generated RSA model, this divergence is found accept-
able and might be caused by simpliﬁcations in the veriﬁcation process. The veriﬁcation
can be found in Appendix E.
A full-page image of all parallel coordinate plots can be found in Appendix F. The
.html ﬁles can be found and interacted with through the digital annex, see Appendix
A.
3.6
Sub-conclusion
Throughout this chapter, two methods for generating geometry and structures have
been displayed illustrating the most common channels of model generation in the early
design phases. The graph used for optimizing the structural truss have been displayed
and explained for these methods.
By generating a range of options automatically and subsequently evaluating these it is
possible to assign a design rating aiding the user in choosing the most desirable option.
By the use of genetic algorithms, the time needed to reach a number of optimal solutions
in a large solution space, set up by the user, is possible within a matter of hours.
The graph saves and stores the generated RSA models allowing the user to quickly
enter a design solution and continue calculations and evaluations without delays.
The visualization of the results in an interactive parallel coordinates plot helps quickly
conveying the results to both the user, colleagues and a possible client by displaying
the range of options generated and the consequences of choosing one path over another.
The graphs and scripts are kept simple to enable superusers to engage with the setup
and modify this to speciﬁc needs if the need arises by avoiding a black-box concept
often found in add-ins. A veriﬁcation of the results is kept accessible to the user.
==PAGE:63==
CHAPTER 4
Complex structure - Optimize
topology
This chapter will not contain any identiﬁable content of the geometry, sections or any
other data relating to the structure optimized upon. References are made to Appendix
G containing the information as some content in this chapter is conﬁdential. The case
will be referred to as The Complex Structure throughout this chapter.
Appendix G is therefore not public and only available to the Author, supervisors, and
sensor in a hard-copy. This chapter will contain the work-ﬂow developed in Dynamo
and Python for a genetic optimization of the structure.
The chapter contains an optimization of a complex structure in 3 dimensions with the
adaption of a non-parametric model developed by Sweco in the program Robot Struc-
tural Analysis (RSA). The model is modiﬁed making critical components adaptable in
both topology and section types. The aim is to minimize the amount of steel used in
the project while maintaining the overall visual representation of the structure.
For a description of the complex structure see Appendix G Section G.1.
If a structure is modelled non-parametric (static) the ability to explore multiple de-
sign solutions are hindered as a signiﬁcant eﬀort is needed to perform remodelling and
analysis.
If the model is created parametrically, the solution space is bigger, but still inﬂuenced
by the designer creating the model as the designer chooses the variable parameters
deﬁning the design space. This limit is currently not possible to sidestep and is a
prerequisite for creating parametric models.
The optimization performed in this chapter is a sub-optimization, with the goal of opti-
mizing structural properties and amount of material used. An argument can be made,
that the reduction of steel amount is a reduction of required CO2 this connection is,
however, not investigated further in this thesis.
To optimize a complex structure, the one in charge of the optimization needs to make
decisions and identify areas where optimization can take place and are deemed advan-
tages. By deciding on areas of intervention and investigation, the subsequent, level
of success for the optimization is determined. Typical objectives for optimization are
minimizing stress, deﬂection, weight, cost and embodied energy while maximizing the
repetition of components (Fisher and Sharma, 2010).
45
==PAGE:64==
46
CHAPTER 4. COMPLEX STRUCTURE - OPTIMIZE TOPOLOGY
Steel structures have a direct link between the amount of steel used and both cost
and embodied energy. The deciding factor is the overall weight of the structure while
keeping a high utilization ratio of the steel members. The optimization carried out on
the complex structure is identifying patterns in the geometry and transforming these
into adaptable geometry. This allows for a change of cross sections in these locations
while keeping the remaining part of the structure locked.
The decisions of the areas of the structure that is subject to optimization are taken on
the grounds of the number of bars in each pattern and the heaviest parts of the struc-
ture as can be seen in Figure G.2. By doing so aims to lower the overall weight and
reduce the number of connections, reducing the overall complexity, working towards
a more buildable project. By choosing a limited number of areas of investigation, the
calculation time is kept manageable.
The optimization is performed using Dynamo graphs and Python scripting which are
presented and described throughout this chapter. As in Chapter 3, the ranking of solu-
tions compares the steel strength of each member to the maximum stress the member is
subject to according to Equations 4.1 and 4.2 found in Section 4.5. All data is saved in
both an individual RSA ﬁle of each iteration with a name corresponding to the design
option, and a record is saved to a .csv ﬁle. An overview of all graphs and scripts can
be found in Table 4.1.
Table 4.1: Overview of graphs and scripts developed optimizing the complex structure
topology and material usage

identiﬁable information to the structure will be discussed and evaluated in Appendix G.

Extract geometry and properties from the original
model
The complex structure seen in Figure G.1 in Appendix G provided by Sweco shows a
structure which is not yet adaptable. It is thus diﬃcult to change and optimize upon
the structure caused by the high complexity of the construction. By extracting the
geometry and properties to comma separated ﬁles using Python the raw data can be
manipulated and made adaptable using VP in Dynamo.
If the structure had originally been created using parametric modelling tools, this step
could be skipped, and the connection to RSA in Section 4.3 would be the initial step.

Projecting parts of the structure onto a plane and ﬁltering out duplicate line instances,
the original structural design of the complex structure is maintained, while allowing

of both horizontal- and cross-bracing. This deﬁnes the number of repeated elements in
the structure, allowing for quick changes to the topology of the structure.
The graph shows an example of how a non-adaptable structure can be made adaptable.
The limit of choosing three areas of intervention is based on informed decisions from
the original design, by limiting the intervention areas the computing time is kept rea-
sonable.
If a structure was modelled with the intention of optimization and iteration, the mod-
elling process would look diﬀerent from the one proposed in this chapter, as the model
would be created with the purpose of being adaptable to change. This would ease the
implementation of automatic evaluation and increase the value of the process. However,
as the industry is conservative and the implementation of automation tools is slow the
introduction of systems able to interact with static models are deemed most applicable
to the current AEC industry.
If an adaptable model is to be constructed, it should be implemented early in the de-
sign phase to have the largest impact on the ﬁnished project. The adaptable model

4.2.2
Re-create custom sections
By re-creating custom sections from a database, the original sections are maintained
in all areas of the structure that are not modiﬁed, by doing so, the visual changes are
minimized. The re-creation of custom sections can be seen in Figure 4.5.
A custom Python script is used for reading the extracted data from Section 4.1 and
feeding the names of the sections to the Dynamo node "Bars.LoadSections". Any bar
which is already in a database accessible from RSA is loaded using this build in method,
avoiding the creation of section types already in the RSA section database.
The second Python script subsequently loads all remaining sections from the data of
the ﬁle. The script automatically checks if the section has already been loaded by
comparing the output from the "Bars.LoadSections" node to the sections which are
being created in the active RSA session. By doing so, all sections are recreated in the
active session, including custom steel sections.

4.3
RSA analytical model
Creating the analytical model in RSA using Dynamo is, as mentioned in Section 2.4.7,
sensitive to timing. The creation of the analytical bars is found to successfully create
all bars, only if a maximum of 250 bars is created at a time. As a consequence of
the complex structure having signiﬁcantly more members than 250, a workaround is
developed. The workaround splits up the creation of the analytical bars into separate
steps. A Python script is used for pausing the work-ﬂow for a number of seconds.
The length of the pause is derived by the number of bars to be created according to:
(NumbBars/100)∗2, resulting in a pause of 4 seconds for the creation of 200 members.
The pause allows RSA the time for creating the necessary members prior to the graph
continuing, thus avoiding possible modelling failures. The simple Python script can be
seen below:
1 import
c l r
2 import time
3 time . sleep (IN [ 1 ] )
4 OUT = IN [ 0 ]
4.3.1
Deﬁne sections, releases, materials, supports, gamma and loads

4.4. OPTIMIZATION USING OPTIMO
55
The automatic evaluation of the structure is performed using tolerances set by the user
with the input and output shown in Table 4.7. The tolerances can be altered based on
the type of structure being designed and can be used to account for safety coeﬃcients.
The evaluation of the design option is performed by applying penalties to the original
self-weight if the stress ratio is either above or below a given user-deﬁned utilization
ratio. The members below the ratio are usually given a smaller penalty than the ones
above it, as over-stressed members are not tolerated, while a high utilization ratio
works towards a more optimized structure.
Table 4.7: Dynamo input (left), .csv output (right)
Dynamo input
.csv output

Iteration number
IN[1] = Penalty weight min
Current date and time
IN[2] = Penalty weight max
Bar ID
IN[3] = Min ratio
Stress value
IN[4] = Max ratio
Maximum deﬂection
IN[5] = Cross brace section name
Self-weight
IN[6] = Number of cross braces
Cross brace section name
IN[7] = Horizontal brace section name
Number of cross braces
IN[8] = Number of horizontal braces
Horizontal brace section name
IN[9] = Main section
Number of horizontal braces
IN[10] = Stress
Calculated weight-score
IN[11] = Maximum deﬂection
Number of over-stressed members
IN[12] = Weight
IN[13] = Steel strength
IN[14] = Bar ID
4.4
Optimization using Optimo
As described in Section 3.4 using Optimo requires the creation of custom nodes. The
custom node is speciﬁc to the optimization which is going to be performed, as all actions
are incorporated into the custom node. By deﬁning varying inputs and outputs the
core node can be used as a function that can evaluate multiple options.
An advantage of using custom nodes is a speed increase for the overall graph. The
Author has observed a speed increase for the complex structure with an execution time
going from 7 minutes prior to being wrapped as a custom node to 2 minutes after being
packed into a custom node. The speed is thus increased by 350%. The total execution
time will depend on the complexity of the structure evaluated, although the execution
time will generally be lowered by packing complex graphs into custom nodes.
4.4.1
Core node
The core node can be seen in Figure 4.8. The node contains a combination of Python
scripts and Dynamo nodes as shown in Figure 4.9.
The core node works as the modelling, calculation and evaluation engine of the work-

ﬂow, this graph generates a structural model from the input provided, assigns corre-
sponding boundary conditions and calculates the project within the engine of RSA.
Figure 4.8: Core node for the complex structure containing all nodes shown in Figure 4.9
When the project has been successfully calculated the results are read and evaluated
within a Python script recording the results in a .csv ﬁle. A single weight-score is
returned deﬁning the overall ﬁtness of the generated design option.

4.4.3
Optimo
The Dynamo graph shown in Figure 4.12 is developed for running and controlling the
optimization. It is simple in design and requires few inputs from the user. The graph
allows the user to run an MOO on a system and to set upper- and lower limits for
the optimization deﬁning the design space. The user can also choose a population and
generation size. The larger the population and the more generations used, the more
exact the result, on the account of processing time.
If an acceptable solution is not found at the end of a run, the user can re-initiate
the optimization starting from the end-point of the previous optimization.
This is
achieved by connecting the data from the Python script at the top of the graph to the
population-list input of the NSGA_II_AssignFitnessFuncResults-node and the arg1 of
the Function.Apply node.

4.5. RESULTS
59
4.5
Results
The problem of a complex structure proposed in this chapter is optimized by making
changes to topology and section sizes.
The structure is subject to wind, live and
dead loads and an automated work-ﬂow have been developed analysing and ranking
the design options by use of a ﬁtness score. Each structural member in the complex
structure is evaluated in regards to the maximum stress in the member and the steel
type used in the same member. By the utilization ratio, it is possible to add a penalty
to the design for each member that is either above or below a user-controlled ratio. The
primary algorithmic loop used for creating one generation of the complex structure can
be seen in Figure 4.13
Figure 4.13: Algorithmic loop, creating one generation of the complex structure
Applying the genetic optimization algorithm described in this chapter can generally be
expressed as follows:
Minimize:
Ws =
n
X
i=1
βi + αi
(4.1)
αi = max(0, ((δ∗
i > δmax →x = pmax) ∧(δ∗
i < δmin →x = pmin)))
(4.2)
Where:
Ws = Weight-score of design option
n = Total number of bars
βi = Bar weight
αi = Penalty weight applied to βi
δ∗
i = Stress ratio for βi
δmax = Maximum allowed stress ratio
pmax = Penalty weight for over-stressed members
δmin = Minimum allowed stress ratio
pmin = Penalty weight for under-stressed members
==PAGE:78==
60
CHAPTER 4. COMPLEX STRUCTURE - OPTIMIZE TOPOLOGY
The number of adaptable bars and sections are variable in the evolutionary process
while applied load-sizes, supports, and releases are ﬁxed. Issues of buckling are rec-
ognized but not deemed applicable in a preliminary evaluation, as the introduction of
buckling analysis would increase the calculation time signiﬁcantly. Buckling is thus not
included in the current analysis.
An initial population of ﬁve with two iterations is chosen due to restrictions of cal-
culation power and issues with a crashing Dynamo after approximately ten functions
evaluated. This is solved by an automatic recording of the population and ﬁtness at the
end of the run and re-initiating using the evolved population as the initial population.
The generation of 10 iterations takes approximately 15 minutes on a PC with Intel Core
i5-6300U 2.4GHz and 8GB RAM. A generation of 10 iterations produces approximately
52.000 data-points used for evaluating and visualizing the generated design options.
A sample of initial designs automatically generated can be observed in Figure G.3
in Appendix G showing a weight reduction of up to 20%.
The current version of the graphs and scripts presented in this chapter is a proto-
type. They can be used for analysing and optimizing upon a structural system such as
the complex structure. The generated design options can further be used for detailed
analysis as structural RSA models are saved upon each iteration. By presenting the
user with data on a variety of design options it is possible to choose a promising design
and utilize the generated model instantly as shown in Figure G.4 for further analysis.
4.6
Sub-conclusion
"If you try and take a cat apart to see how it works, the ﬁrst thing you have on your
hands is a non-working cat." (Adams, 2002)
The chapter showed an approach to optimizing upon a complex structure in 3-dimensional
space using GA optimization through VP in Dynamo. By automating the process of
design evaluation and convergence towards a more optimal design in regards to mate-
rial usage, strength, and build-ability a wider knowledge of possibilities related to the
design is achieved. It is shown how a non-parametric model can be converted to an
adaptable one using the interaction between VP and RSA.
Depending on the design stage the design objectives will change, and the area of inves-
tigations will likewise change. In the early design, the objective of exploring multiple
possible design alternatives in predeﬁned areas might be the goal, while later stages
search for a speciﬁc solution within a narrower solution space. The process proposed
in this chapter is intended for the early design exploration rather than the later, more
narrow, solution search.
==PAGE:79==
CHAPTER 5
Optimizing loading lines
This chapter investigate a steel structure modelled by the Author representing a ﬁve-
story building as seen in Figure 5.1. The structure consists of columns, beams, and
braces modelled in Revit.
The structure is modelled non-parametric in Revit. This means that changes are cum-
bersome and demanding, this illustrates the current practice of modelling BIM projects
and the possibility of introducing preliminary evaluation on non-parametric structures.
In this chapter, an automatic change is applied to the loading lines, and ﬂoor heights
of the structure. Through an automated analysis, the most optimal placement for the
individual ﬂoors and loading lines are found. The engineer and architect can use this
knowledge to discover possibilities not considered in the initial design and for uncov-
ering new design options. An overview of the developed graphs and scripts for this
optimization can be seen in Table 5.1.
Figure 5.1: Revit steel structure in 5 stories

2.3 Genetic optimization
Dynamo
Performing the optimization using Dynamo and Optimo
5.1
Analytical model
To utilize the Revit geometry in Dynamo, a consistent analytical model is assumed to
be present as described by Hejnfelt, 2016. The analytical model is a simpliﬁed rep-
resentation of the structural model and consists of analytical stick elements holding
information of geometry, material properties and loads. The analytical model of the
structure can be seen in Figure 5.2.
Stick elements have solely a start- and an end-point the geometric properties are lim-
ited compared to structural elements in Revit. The analytical model is likewise used
for structural calculations in third-party software using Add-ins for e.g. FEM Design
or RSA. These connections, however, do not allow for adaptable parameters and can
not be used for automated optimization of a design.
Figure 5.2: Analytical model in Revit
==PAGE:81==
5.2. ADAPTABLE LOADING LINES AND FLOOR HEIGHTS
63
If a consistent analytical model is not present, a correction of the analytical model can
be carried out automatically through VP. The analytical model is in most industry
cases not connected correctly as discussed by Hejnfelt, 2016 who proposes a method
for automated correction of analytical models in Revit using VP.
By considering the analytical model as a set of start- and endpoints the geometry
becomes manageable. Using VP software controlled changes can be implemented on
the structural layout using loops, as all required information for structural analysis is
available in the analytical model elements.
5.2
Adaptable loading lines and ﬂoor heights
To allow modiﬁcation of the geometry enabling automatic changes to the loading lines,
a Python script has been written handling the movement of all nodes along the same
coordinates. Inside the Python script shown in Figure 5.3 the points at grid 2, 3 and
4 for both x and y directions along with the height of all levels, except from the entry
level, can be moved by user-deﬁned inputs.
The perimeter and bottom ﬂoor is kept static as the overall footprint of the building
is not intended for changes during automatic genetic optimization.
(a) Side view, red arrows represent adaptable parameters (top in x & y-directions) (b) Python code
Figure 5.3: Python script (b) used for modifying analytical nodes along loading lines (a)
The inputs of the script shown in Figure 5.3b consist of the original points, the changes
applied and a tolerance input. The tolerance has been included to allow the user of
the graph to easily change tolerance without having to interact with the scripting envi-
ronment of Python appealing to non-coders. The script can be found in Appendix C.
==PAGE:82==
64
CHAPTER 5. OPTIMIZING LOADING LINES
With the objective of building a graph that requires as little resources as possible,
the decision of including all the geometry conversion and adaptions inside a Python
node allowing for a more eﬃcient graph. As the Python code is nested into the graph
the apparent complexity of the graph is kept low for users with little or no scripting
experience. An illustration of the required inputs and subsequent output of the Python
script can be seen in Figure 5.4.
Figure 5.4: Changing the location of start- and endpoints in Dynamo, script 1.1 in Table 5.1
The geometric changes to the loading lines are made in meters with a distance either
+ or −from the original location in the x-,y- and z-direction. By solely performing the
changes on the geometry in Dynamo the Revit model remains unchanged, and work
as the starting point for each iteration. This allows for the comparison of results and
assurance of continuity in the models.
5.3
Automated variable load-application
Load sizes for the structure is an area load which is subdivided along the loading lines.
The live loads are determined according to (DS/EN 1991-1-1, 2007, Table 6.2) deﬁning
a live load for oﬃce buildings from 2.0kN
m2 to 3.0kN
m2 the live load used for calculations
is thus determined to be 2.5kN
m2 .
The snow load has been calculated according to DS/EN 1991-1-3, 2007 and DS/EN
==PAGE:83==
5.3. AUTOMATED VARIABLE LOAD-APPLICATION
65
1991-1-3 DK:NA, 2015 as the snow load is subject to regional changes. The calculation
of the snow load can be seen in Equation 5.1 (DS/EN 1991-1-3, 2007, Equation 5.1).
s = µi · Ce · Ct · sk = 0.8 · 1.0 · 1.0 · 1.0 = 0.8kN
m2
(5.1)
The structure would likewise be subject to wind loading. The wind load is assumed
to be taken by the concrete core which is outside the scope of the analysis. The wind
load is therefore not applied to the structure, if the wind load were to be calculated
this would be done according to DS/EN 1991-1-4, 2007 and DS/EN 1991-1-4 DK:NA,
2010.
Figure 5.5: Applying area loads according to DS/EN 1991-1-1, 2007, DS/EN 1991-1-3, 2007 and
DS/EN 1991-1-3 DK:NA, 2015 over variable lengths, graph 1.2 in Table 5.1
Figure 5.5 shows the Dynamo graph created for assigning variable load sizes depending
on loading line placement. Point by point explanation of the graph can be found in
Table 5.2.
Table 5.2: Graph explanation - Applying variable loads over an area
1. Get the location of loading lines and length between lines
2. Input load values according to DS/EN
3. Retrieve analytical bars organized by placement
4. Calculate the load value for loading line matching analytical bar placement
5. Apply load to analytical bars varying with sizes calculated in step 4
The analytical bars corresponding to each loading line is used for dividing the area
load onto the structural loading lines. The varying lengths are taken into account in
this evaluation.
The input of live- and snow-load is made easy by allowing the user to input the area-
load directly in the optimization graph avoiding the need of performing changes to the
custom nodes if an alternative load is required.

66
CHAPTER 5. OPTIMIZING LOADING LINES
5.4
Genetic optimization through Dynamo
The genetic optimization carried out in this chapter works to determine the optimal
location of loading lines and individual ﬂoor heights. This result in the lowest possible
overall stress and deﬂection of the structure compared to the maximum allowable stress
and deﬂection.
The structural evaluation for deﬂection is carried out according to DS/EN 1993-1-1
DK:NA, 2013 as seen in Table 5.3.
Table 5.3: Maximum allowable deﬂection (DS/EN 1993-1-1 DK:NA, 2013)
Structural component
Max allowable deﬂection
Beams in ﬂoors
l
400
Beams in roof
l
200
Braces
h
150
Columns
h
300
The actual deﬂection can thus be compared to the maximum allowable one ﬁnding a
utilization ratio for each design option generated by the algorithm. The ratio together
with the utilization ratio of stresses in each bar is combined to form the ﬁnal design
score deﬁning the ﬁtness of the design. The lower the overall score, the better the
solution.
The rating of the designs is handled in a Python script in the core node. The script
ﬁlters out solutions outside realistic scenarios and records the data in a .csv ﬁle for
later data processing. Likewise, all structural models are saved to the user’s computer
easing the further modelling task by removing the step of having to model the structure
in RSA. The rating of the designs is carried out according to Equation 5.2 and 5.3.
Minimize:
DR =
n
X
i=1
βi + αi
(5.2)
αi = defi
mad
βi = Si
Y si
(5.3)
Where:
DR = Design rating for current design
n = Total number of bars
αi = Deﬂection ratio
defi = Max deﬂection of member
mad = Max allowable deﬂection according to Table 5.3
βi = Stress ratio
Si = Maximum stress in member
Y si = Yield strength of member
==PAGE:85==
5.4. GENETIC OPTIMIZATION THROUGH DYNAMO
67
5.4.1
Core node
The core node handles all interaction with RSA. The ﬁrst step is, as described in Section
5.2 to modify the structure according to the input parameters. Secondly boundary
conditions are applied to the structure and materials and sections are deﬁned. As the
purpose of this optimization is to discover the most optimal placements for the loading
lines the cross section is kept unchanging. Subsequently, snow-, dead- and live-loads
are applied to the structure, and the data is passed to the calculation node performing
the structural calculation in RSA.
The resulting maximum deﬂection and stress is retrieved and passed to a Python script
for evaluation and recording.
Figure 5.6: Core node for genetic optimization of loading lines, graph 2.1 in Table 5.1
Figure 5.6 shows the Dynamo graph created for optimizing loading lines for a steel
frame structure in Revit using GA’s. Point by point explanation of the graph can be
found in Table 5.4.
Table 5.4: Graph explanation - Core node for performing genetic optimization of loading lines
1. Deﬁne input for custom node
2. Capture geometry from Revit and convert from mm to m
3. Python script adapting geometry to changes from input
4. Create a geometric structure in Dynamo geometry
5. Load sections into RSA
6. Create analytical bars in RSA
7. Identify supporting nodes
8. Capture loading lines lengths
9. Set boundary conditions for structure (releases, material, sections, supports)
10. Create load cases
11. Apply dead-, snow- and live-loads as described in Section 5.3
12. Collect all information and prepare for calculation
13. Deﬁne a naming standard for save of the RSA model and perform the calculation
14. Retrieve calculation results for deﬂection and stress for each bar
15. Evaluate and record results in Python script
16. Output results to the output port of custom node
The custom node is closed, and interaction with the core graph is intended only for
superusers and programmers. The issues of "black-box" functions are avoided by dis-
playing each step and allow changes and modiﬁcations by users with Dynamo and/or
Python knowledge.
==PAGE:86==
68
CHAPTER 5. OPTIMIZING LOADING LINES
5.4.2
Fitness node
The ﬁtness node enables the evaluation of each population member allowing for genetic
optimization as an entire population can be passed by the population list input. This
way the core node is executed for each member of the current population. The ﬁtness
node is able to return a design rating for each option evaluated.
Figure 5.7: Fitness node evaluate population for optimization of loading lines, graph 2.2 in Table 5.1
Figure 5.7 shows the Dynamo graph created for evaluation of each member of the
current population and executing the core graph. Point by point explanation of the
graph can be found in Table 5.5.
Table 5.5: Graph explanation - Fitness node for loading lines
1. Deﬁne input for custom node
2. Round population members to 2 digits and execute the graph for each member
3. Core node as described in Section 5.4.1
4. Output design rating for each member of the current population as a list
5.4.3
Optimo
The Optimo graph works to discover the combination of parameters that will gain the
lowest possible design rating as returned by the Fitness node. The more generations
and the larger populations are used, the more exact the results will be. A conscious
decision is to be taken by the user of the desired precision as a high precision will
lead to longer computation time. Using a Python node a sound is played as the graph
ﬁnishes alerting the user of the completion. By doing so, the optimization can run in
the background without the need for checking if it has completed.
==PAGE:87==
5.5. RESULTS
69
Figure 5.8: The main graph for genetic optimization of loading lines, graph 2.3 in Table 5.1
Figure 5.8 shows the Dynamo graph created for applying genetic optimization algo-
rithms to the Revit structure displayed in Figure 5.1. Point by point explanation of
the graph can be found in Table 5.6.
Table 5.6: Graph explanation - Main graph used for genetic optimization of loading lines
1. Input options for the user, specifying population, generation, lower-, upper-limits,
save locations for models and .csv data
2. Possibility for the user to initiate from previously saved generation
3. Fitness custom node as described in Section 5.4.2
4. Generating and applying initial population
5. Main evaluation creating new generations and applying elitist mutations
6. Save current generation to .csv for possible later use
5.5
Results
The evaluation is carried out by running through a number of generations with the
developed dynamo graph. The user can easily make changes to the optimization pa-
rameters if desired. By doing so, the optimal placement of loading lines in the structure
can be automatically located.
The computational time required for generating 250 possible solutions and extracting
approximately 1.5 million data points using a population of 50 and 5 generations, takes
approximately 10 hours on a computer with Intel Core 2.4 GHz processor and 8 GB
RAM. A memory leak has been identiﬁed resulting in an increasing iteration time for
each iteration. Disregarding this the same optimization can be completed in 1.25 hours
using the same system conﬁguration.
By generating a single design rating for each solution, it is possible to bring the MOO
to a single number and use the genetic algorithm to approach a Pareto optimal solu-
tion.
A display of the individual movements of each loading line is displayed in Figure 5.9a,
5.9b and 5.9c illustrating the complexity of evaluating a problem in 11 dimensions.
Each color represents a parameter subject to change. Discovering a connection be-
tween each option quickly becomes diﬃcult, and the risk of being overwhelmed by
information is present.

By using the results found in this chapter the user can make information-based decisions

5.6
Sub-conclusion
Throughout this chapter, the graph has been developed using a GA for determining
the optimal placement of loading lines in a steel frame structure.
Automating the
generation of design alternatives gives the user a range of options and subsequent
inﬂuence on the structural properties of the building. The GA oﬀers a range of optimal
non-dominated solutions discovered and visualized.
The original design is found with a design rating of 144.78 while the most optimal
design found running a population of 50 and 10 generations is found to be 140.12
lowering the design rating by 4.66. The results is a design with 0 over-stressed or
-deﬂected members by moving slightly upon loading lines.
The advantage of using multi-criteria optimization as a tool in the early design phase
is the ability to discover solutions beyond those of experienced designers. Exploring
the solution space within a known set of parameters quickly discovering sub-optimal
non-dominated solutions.
==PAGE:93==
CHAPTER 6
Discussion
In this chapter the VP work-ﬂow developed, the inﬂuence on the current industry work-
ﬂow, and the need for information ownership, and placement of responsibilities will be
discussed. Finally, the importance for interoperability of software and the underlying
knowledge of the user will be discussed.
Using existing models for performing multi-objective optimization at an early stage
gives architects and engineers the ability to explore multiple design options using a
minimum of resources. The primary beneﬁt of this is that the structures are automati-
cally remodelled, and iteration-models are saved which can later be used for veriﬁcation
and continued work on a solution without delays. However, as the initial setup of the
graph and visualization script requires a minimum knowledge of Dynamo and Python.
It may be diﬃcult for designers to achieve the full beneﬁts of the genetic optimiza-
tion developed in this thesis without the intervention of an engineer with Dynamo and
Python-knowledge prior to using the tool.
Likewise, a basic knowledge of population and generation size is an advantage to most
eﬃciently explore promising solutions within a user-deﬁned solution range. This part
of the process can be standardized within an organization depending on the required
level of detail needed in the solutions.
By introducing the structural analysis in an early phase, it is possible to spot pitfalls
and work towards a more harmonious design guiding the designer towards statically
sound solutions while retaining design freedom. By allowing the engineer the ability to
be ﬂexible in the design space, the need for a compromise on either structural eﬃciency
nor aesthetics needs to be present yielding better and cheaper structures.
During the process of writing the thesis, two presentations have been performed pre-
senting the ongoing work. One presentation was performed for Karlsruhe University of
Technology on their visit at DTU and the second was performed for the Department
of BIM Application Development located in Sweco Finland. These presentations have
worked in verifying the developed work-ﬂow and assure the relevance of the research
by introducing it to the industry. The presentations revealed a general interest from
the industry in the introduction of automation for reducing the number of proﬁles and
connections used in designs, as this generally will reduce the overall cost of a design.
75
==PAGE:94==
76
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION
6.1
Work-ﬂow change
It is a known fact that the current work-ﬂow in the construction industry leans towards
a conservative adoption of automation, and many tasks are still undertaken using hand
calculations and spreadsheets. This is partly related to the extensive work required
in performing modelling and analysis in FEM software and the inability of handling
design changes in an eﬃcient way.
Utilizing the work performed in this thesis, it is possible to semi-automate parts of
the modelling and analysis steps, automatically evaluating the results at an early stage
using the FEM software RSA. The work in this thesis is a sub-optimization where areas
like MEP, sustainability, etc. are not currently considered. However, by establishing
automated Dynamo work-ﬂows, it is possible to include the analysis of other ﬁelds of
interest in the developed setup. By doing so, results can be gained along a Pareto front
without the need of changing the overall setup of the VP work-ﬂow.
The ability to connect directly to RSA through the API enables the process of au-
tomation, limiting the required user interaction. It was found, that by giving the user
control of loads, boundary conditions, and design space, a more user-friendly and us-
able work-ﬂow could be developed.
The use of Dynamo for carrying out optimization tasks compared to build-in add-ins for
Revit oﬀers higher ﬂexibility. Developing an open work-ﬂow oﬀers the responsible user
the possibility of verifying the inner workings of the graph avoiding a black-box, aid-
ing in the documentation of the developed work-ﬂow. Interacting with the scripts and
graphs does require a minimum of coding and graphing skills, but as VP is user-friendly
compared to conventional scripting methods, most users will be able to interact with
and customize the graph. Using add-ins, the users would need to develop workarounds
if minor changes are needed to the work-ﬂow removing transparency from the interac-
tion.
The possibility of interacting with the graphs and codes introduces the demand for
a predeﬁned contractual agreement in the early design phases. The agreement works
to determine the responsibilities of the involved parties for the information included in
the models and subsequent analysis. The need for the introduction of data-ownership
is caused by the increase in available information along with an increase in expenses
in the early phases. This increase in expenses should be compared to the beneﬁts of
using the developed work-ﬂow for optimization and later savings by reducing the risk
of unexpected errors and a decrease in later analysis time. These topics need to be
addressed in the initial contracts incorporating the use of optimization tools at an early
stage.
It should likewise be understood by anyone using the software that the results devel-
oped are based on preliminary analysis and should be treated as such. The user should
take the output as proposals guiding towards promising areas of intervention rather
than deciding upon a speciﬁc path. By assisting in the creation of a symbiosis between
the designer and the engineer a more ﬂexible co-operation throughout the project can
be achieved.
It is vital, in setting up a model for genetic optimization using the NSGA-II algorithm,
that a concurrent and sound analytical model is present as demonstrated in Chapter
5. The modelling and initial setup require basic knowledge of structural properties to
==PAGE:95==
6.2. MODEL MATURITY, LIMITATIONS, AND IMPLEMENTATION
77
generate a proper optimization work-ﬂow. It is likewise important that the deﬁnition
and use of weight-scores are set up by a user with knowledge of the structural workings
of the design. The method of MOO used is eﬀective in ﬁnding a solution that satisﬁes
multiple criteria discovering non-dominated solutions as shown in Figure 5.10 to any
type of structure or problem.
A list of pros and cons is displayed in Table 6.1 for the developed work-ﬂows.
Pros
Generates multiple design options from static model
Flexible in solution range
NSGA-II generates non-dominated solutions in solution range
Repetitive usage on projects is easy once setup
Can handle multiple objectives
Easily visualized solutions
Cons
Require minimal knowledge of VP and Python for setup
Needs slight customization to ﬁt individual projects
General structural knowledge required by the user for setup
Table 6.1: Advantages and disadvantages of developed work-ﬂow
It is evident from the interviews Section 2.3 and literary study Section 1.4 that a user
does not wish to have a single optimal solution presented. The user would instead prefer
to be presented with a solution space with a clear ranking of the possible solutions
giving a bigger freedom of design. This allows the user to freely explore the design
alternatives keeping control of the decision process. By generating an interactive visual
representation of the possible solutions with an explicit score/rating, a quick overview
of possible solution paths for the project is given to the user aiding design decisions.
6.2
Model maturity, limitations, and implementation
Model maturity
To perform a structural optimization of a model it has been found that several factors
need to be present for a successful model generation and evaluation. Before setting
up the optimization work-ﬂow, the original model from which information is accessed
should contain information about a consistent analytical model, releases, supports,
loads, and materials. This information should be a part of best practice when creating
BIM models and decisions regarding these areas should be performed by a user with
knowledge within structural engineering as incorrect results may otherwise be obtained.
This requirement will result in the necessity of a higher level of information (LOI) at an
early stage than what is the current practice, as much early stage modelling is currently
performed with regards to the visual 3D model only. Including the structural engineer
at an early stage would help assure the required information is available as described
in the interviews Appendix D.
==PAGE:96==
78
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION
Limitations
Throughout the development of the work-ﬂow in this thesis, limitations in the interop-
erability between the software have been found in both speed limitations and memory
handling when working between Dynamo and RSA. The interaction between the soft-
ware causes a memory leak in the analysis resulting in increasing ﬁle size. The size
grows with 4 KB/evaluation giving in an increase of 2 MB pr. 500 evaluations. This
can result in signiﬁcant ﬁle sizes for substantial optimization’s. Likewise, a speed de-
crease in operations has been found, the speed of the work-ﬂow decrease linearly. A
plot of the increase in iteration time can be seen in Figure 6.1 displaying a projected
completion time of 7.2 days for 1100 iterations of the loading line problem from Chap-
ter 5. If the process were to run, disregarding the increase in iteration time, successful
completion of 1100 iterations could be reached in 5.5 hours. It is therefore important
that future developments of the work-ﬂow investigates and deals with the increase in
iteration time.
Figure 6.1: Increase in iteration time for optimization
The increase in iteration time is possibly related to the memory leak in the RAM
discussed earlier. With a better interaction between Dynamo and RSA, a faster recre-
ation of models is possible and thus a faster optimization and visualization of possible
designs. In this thesis, a workaround to the iteration times is proposed involving a
temporary save of the current population and re-initiating the process using the saved
population as the initial seed. This option, however, requires the user to terminate
Revit, Dynamo, and RSA and re-launch the applications.
In section 4.3 a limitation is discussed causing a maximum of 250 analytical bars
to be created at a time. Improved interoperability of the software could, likewise, com-
bat this limitation. In the same section, a workaround has been proposed allowing for
==PAGE:97==
6.2. MODEL MATURITY, LIMITATIONS, AND IMPLEMENTATION
79
the creation of an increased number of analytical bars resulting in a slightly increased
model generation time.
Throughout the thesis, Python has been used for evaluating and analyzing parts of
the work-ﬂow. Using Python has been beneﬁcial compared to scripting with compiled
programming languages such as C#, as the scripting in Python provides instant feed-
back in Dynamo and is more readable for people with basic understanding of coding
languages. This enables the development of scripts that can more easily be modiﬁed
and customized by a bigger group of users, as the Python script is available inside Dy-
namo. In addition, most libraries utilizing GA’s are created using Python. It should
be noted that scripting in C# would provide for slightly faster and more robust nodes
in comparison to those of Python.
Implementation
The implementation of the developed work-ﬂow into the industry requires a broad adop-
tion of BIM tools supporting the proposed work-ﬂow with the required information. By
adopting automation and evaluation into the industry, a requirement for a minimum
knowledge within VP and scripting would be required from the users. Therefore, this
should be implemented as a requirement already at the universities.
It is vital that both management and personnel have a focus on concurrency within
data management and responsibility of data to achieve correct models as discussed
earlier.
The AEC industry is vast, and the introduction of new work-ﬂows and changes are
time-consuming and diﬃcult. For many years there has been a focus on BIM in the
industry, and as a result, the willingness to invest and attempt implementation of new
workﬂows are made more accessible, as people with interests within BIM and VDC
have reached deciding positions allowing an easier introduction of automation tools.
If the developed work-ﬂow is implemented successfully into the industry, it could oﬀer
the company in question the ability to include the client in the decision-making at an
earlier stage, calculate more design options, and oﬀer a general edge in competitions.
The implementation of automated structural analysis and evaluation into the industry
work-ﬂow could thus result in an increased income for a company.
==PAGE:98==
==PAGE:99==
CHAPTER 7
Conclusions
"We shape our buildings; thereafter they shape us."
Winston Churchill
This thesis aims to investigate the possibility of automating the early structural anal-
ysis of steel structures and oﬀer a range of optimized solutions to the user, aiding the
early design decisions. A computational method is developed in the Visual Program-
ming tool Dynamo bridging the analysis tool Robot Structural Analysis and parametric
design through a combination of visual programming in Dynamo and textual program-
ming in Python. The combination of programming- and analysis-software allows for a
more interactive approach to structural design which to the knowledge of the Author is
sparsely used in the industry today. The method is tested on three cases consisting of a
2D truss, a complex 3D structure, and a 3D steel frame building. The method presented
can aid companies through the automatic generation of multiple non-dominated design
alternatives for a client and allow for companies to remain competitive in a digitalized
industry.
This idea supports the mindset behind Virtual Design and Construction
(VDC).
The developed work-ﬂow aims to generate synergy between structure and form by
intelligently evaluating design options within a user-deﬁned solution-space by using ge-
netic algorithms for generating eﬃcient structures from advanced architectural designs.
The cases analysed in this thesis shows the advantage of ﬁnding multiple non-dominated
solutions over a single optimal solution. By coding an automatic visualization of the
results in Python, using the packages Plotly and Pandas, the designer is allowed the
freedom of exploring the generated solutions discovering the most promising designs.
This allows for future additions to the developed work-ﬂow including the evaluation
of design conditions within other ﬁelds. This would allow the generation of truly opti-
mized designs.
To use the developed work-ﬂow either a working Robot Structural Analysis model
or an analytically correct Revit model need to be present. This means that informa-
tion regarding material properties, sections, loads, and boundary conditions need to
be present by failing to achieve this in the initial model the generation of erroneous
results should be assumed.
81
==PAGE:100==
82
CHAPTER 7. CONCLUSIONS
Throughout this thesis, possibilities and limitations to the developed work-ﬂow have
been presented. It should be noted that analyses carried out in the early stages are
preliminary and detailed connection results are not considered. The results obtained
from the developed work-ﬂow should be used as a guide, not a ﬁnite solution aiding
the designer in making a decision using analytical results and information rather than
rules of thumb.
The work presented in this thesis provides a computational method for automatic
optimization of the structural design. The method does, however, not replace the en-
gineer or designer but aids in the decision making by handling much of the structural
evaluations and calculation. By automatically iterating towards a set of non-dominated
optimal solutions the possibility of freeing up human resources allows the involved par-
ties additional time for working on other challenges in the project.
The implementation of the work proposed in this thesis allows for a more collaborative
environment for the client, architects, contractors, and engineers bringing structural
analysis into the early design phase.
==PAGE:101==
CHAPTER 8
Future Work
This chapter describes a few ideas considered for future extensions of the developed
workﬂow and possible oﬀspring’s are suggested for future research. The ideas represent
the ones assessed as being practically possible and are not listed in any particular order.
Include detailed connection design
Including the detailed connection design in the work-ﬂow, the possibility of reducing
the material usage further and save time on calculations. By reducing the total number
of diﬀerent connections would additionally lower the cost of the structure into the later
stages of the building process. The work developed in this thesis could likewise be
used in combination with other tools. An example is the development of using AI for
calculating connection design in Tekla structures currently being developed in Sweco
Finland. The combination of connection design and general optimization of the build-
ing structure would yield faster and better production of projects.
Evaluate other engineering areas
By coupling the developed work-ﬂow with sub-optimization tasks within other engineer-
ing areas such as space-planning, MEP, lighting, LCA, or ﬁre-safety a truly optimal
solution spanning multiple ﬁelds could be obtained ensuring an even better collabora-
tion between the engineers, architects, and contractors.
Expansion to composite- and concrete structures
By expanding the work-ﬂow and allowing it to work with multiple materials makes it
applicable to more cases in the industry where buildings are more likely to be built
using a mix of material. Evaluating the structural interaction between the material is
essential to analyse the performance of the building design successfully. The method
put forth in this thesis can be used to further develop the work-ﬂow to include a span
of materials and types of structures.
Include buckling analysis
If an automatic work-ﬂow is evaluating the design in the later stages of the design
process, several additional evaluation criteria should be added to the work-ﬂow. These
include analysis of buckling, dynamics, and connection speciﬁc analysis. By extending
the work-ﬂow into the later phases, it would require a higher level of continuous docu-
mentation of the results and investigations into a more narrow solution space.
83
==PAGE:102==
84
CHAPTER 8. FUTURE WORK
Rewrite solution as an add-in
By transforming the solution into a black box, a speed increase connected with running
the process entirely script-based could be an advantage. It is, however, possible that
issues may arise from removing the transparency of the work-ﬂow. By transforming
the work-ﬂow into a single add-on for Revit, it would be possible for anyone to use
the tool. This would require a higher level of built-in knowledge as the add-in should
partly replace the engineer otherwise needed for ﬁlling in the gaps.
Update the developed work-ﬂow for future software versions
The work-ﬂow developed is made as generic as possible by using mainly build in nodes
and integrated Python coding. Future updates to software, such as the release of Dy-
namo 2.0 and future version of Revit and RSA, will result in a need for adjustments to
the developed graphs and scripts as the connection between the various software might
be changed. Two examples to mention is the change from Dynamo 1.3.x to 2.x and the
change from Python 2.x to 3.x resulting in a change in syntax; this leads to the need
for a correction of the graphs and scripts.
Use interaction with alternative analysis software
By investigating the possibility of creating a similar work-ﬂow interacting with alter-
native analysis software such as FEM Design, comparisons could be made evaluating
the advantages and disadvantages of using one software over the other in addition to
making the developed work-ﬂow applicable for a more signiﬁcant number of users.

20 # Removing the
outer
parts
of
the
structure
keeping
the
structure
outer
walls
s t a t i c .
21 def remove_min_max( l s t ) :

24
Values . remove (min( Values ) )
25
return
l i s t ( Values )

28

29

30
Values . remove (min( Values ) )
31

l i s t ( Values )

value
fo r x , y and z

APPENDIX D
Interview Answers
D.1
Interview with Keita Sato - Nihon Sekkei
Title: Structural Engineer
Date: 04-Apr-2018
How often does major project (design) changes take place after the early design phase,
and what are the most common cause for these changes?
There are many reasons for the design change during design phases. (For instance : the
request from the client, the judgment from estimated costs, the change of the economy,
etc...)
On what grounds are designs evaluated and “rated” during the early design?
It depends on the project and takes place very often in Japan.
How do you see the possibilities of using a semi-automated process of performing prelim-
inary analysis on early project proposals and thus open for the possibilities for making
more informed decisions at an early stage?
In my opinion, using optimization for early design phases is very eﬀective because we
can get many design options and choose the optimum answer.
However the optimum answer is not the real “optimized” solution. From a view point
of an objective function that you deﬁne, the answer might be the best but from others
it might not the best.
Since we should design architectures taking many evaluation factors into consideration,
the optimized answer could be used as just a draft model. Then we should brush up
and arrange it many times.
That said, it is very eﬀective to get many design options and change current design
ﬂow using optimization instead of using hand calculations and hand drawings.
How does the project change work-ﬂow look from your perspective from starting a pro-
cess to delivering a ﬁnished project, what are the most common changes etc.?
When you start designing, you will understand the work ﬂow much better.
119
==PAGE:138==
120
APPENDIX D. INTERVIEW ANSWERS
D.2
Interview with René Bjerg Nielsen - Sweco
Title: Konstruktionsingeniør / Fagprojektleder
Date: 19-Mar-2018
Hvor ofte sker der store ændringer i projekter (designmæssigt) efter den tidlige design
fase, og hvad er de mest normale grunde til disse ændringer?
Optimeringerne kan ske på forskellige tidspunkter, og er afhængig af entrepriseformen.
F.eks. i en totalentreprise kan der være ønske om nogle optimeringer tidligere end
normalt på enkelte områder. De mest normale grunde er ift. et ønske fra bygherren,
entreprenøren eller smeden.
Hvordan bliver designs evalueret og ”rangeret” i de tidlige faser?
Det kan f.eks. være en dialog med arkitekt/entreprenør/ingeniør. Så opstiller man
fordele/ulemper for de forskellige design løsninger ift. det enkelte fag.
Hvordan ser du mulighederne for at benytte en semi-automatiseret process til at udføre
overslags-analyser på tidlige design forslag og på den måde gøre det muligt at træﬀe
mere informerede beslutninger tidligt i forløbet?
Det vil være en stor fordel, at kunne optimere denne proces – da man hurtigt kan få
pejlet sig ind på den rigtige løsning.
I hvor høj grad indeholder digitale modeller brugt i de tidlige faser relevante informa-
tioner omkring de vigtigste statiske komponenter (bjælker, søjler, trusses)?
Er dette ift. Robot eller Revit? – I Robot påføres de statiske komponenter de relevante
code parametres ift. design.
Hvordan ser workﬂowet ud i hht. projektændringer fra dit perspektiv, fra starten af et
projekt, til aﬂevering af dette, og hvad er de mest normale ændringer?
Det er meget forskelligt fra projekt til projekt. Det kan både være bygherre ændringer i
form af ønsker. Men kan sandelig også være arkitektændringer. Senere i processen kan
der være at entreprenøren/smeden ønsker nogle konkrete ændringer (typisk samlinger)
for at optimere bygprocessen.
==PAGE:139==
D.3. INTERVIEW WITH THOMAS BO FREDERIKSEN - SWECO
121
D.3
Interview with Thomas Bo Frederiksen - Sweco
Title: Konstruktionsingeniør / Gruppeleder
Date: 10-Apr-2018
Hvor ofte sker der store ændringer i projekter (designmæssigt) efter den tidlige design
fase, og hvad er de mest normale grunde til disse ændringer?
Der sker stort set altid ændringer efter de tidlige designfaser (f. eks. projektforslag).
De væsentligste grunde er ofte:
- Arkitekt ændringer fordi de ikke har været helt færdige, eller fordi de ønsker at
lave nogle ting om.
- Bygherre ønsker. Ofte ændre bygherrens ønske og behov sig også gennem det
meste af projektforløbet
- Entreprenør ønsker. Entreprenøren ønsker tit at ændre ting for at kunne sparre
penge eller tid i udførelsen. Ligeledes sker der tit ændringer når entreprenøren først
får sine underleverandører på plads.
Hvordan bliver designs evalueret og ”rangeret” i de tidlige faser?
Jeg er ikke sikker på at jeg forstår spørgsmålet 100 % korrekt.
Men ofte, så gen-
nemser/gennemgår både bygherre og entreprenør (hvis sådan en er valgt på det tidlige
tidspunkt) designet efter både dispositionsforslag og projektforslag, og godkender disse.
Hvordan ser du mulighederne for at benytte en semi-automatiseret process til at udføre
overslags-analyser på tidlige design forslag og på den måde gøre det muligt at træﬀe
mere informerede beslutninger tidligt i forløbet?
Det er helt sikkert noget der vil blive mere og mere anvendt. Især på komplekse kon-
struktioner, eller på det tidspunkt hvor arkitekten skal vælge bygningens form. Så jeg
er sikker på at det vil blive mere anvendt i fremtiden.
I hvor høj grad indeholder digitale modeller brugt i de tidlige faser relevante informa-
tioner omkring de vigtigste statiske komponenter (bjælker, søjler, trusses)?
Hvis ingeniørfaget er inde over modellerne vil de også indeholde de relevante informa-
tioner vedr. de statiske komponenter.
Hvordan ser workﬂowet ud i hht. projektændringer fra dit perspektiv, fra starten af et
projekt, til aﬂevering af dette, og hvad er de mest normale ændringer?
Som rådgiver er vi de sidste par år blevet meget mere opmærksomme på ændringer
end tidligere. Dette skyldes hovedsageligt at vi bliver presset på vores rådgiverhonorar,
og derfor ikke har timer til at sidde og ændre projektet når det skulle være fastlåst.
Op til dispositionsforslag vil der selvfølgelig være en masse ændringer/forskellige forslag
som en del af designfasen, indtil man får fastlagt hvorledes byggeriet skal se ud. I fasen
fra dispositionsforslag til projektforslag vil der også være ændringer, og det er her man
eksempelvis ﬁnder ud af om man skal bruge stålbjælker eller betonbjælker og lignende.
Når projektforslaget er godkendt, er bygningen i realiteten fastlåst, og de er nu kun
detaljeringen der mangler frem til aﬂeveret hovedprojekt. I denne fase ser vi en del
==PAGE:140==
122
APPENDIX D. INTERVIEW ANSWERS
ændringer, og det er ændringer som koster i vores timeforbrug, hvis vi ikke får betalt
for disse ændringer. I denne fase er det både arkitekt, bygherre og entreprenør der
ønsker ændringer.
Når hovedprojektet er aﬂeveret går vi over i udførelsesfasen. Her er der også ændringer,
og de kommer for det meste fra entreprenøren fordi at de ønsker en billiger eller hur-
tigere løsningsmulighed. Som beskrevet tidligere kommer ændringer i denne fase også
når entreprenøren får endeligt valgt sine underleverandøren. Det kan være at huset
er lavet med kompositbjælker fra Peikko, og så ønsker entreprenøren at ændre til en
anden leverandør af kompositbjælker, eller måske endda betonbjælker. Dette går at vi
skal rette vores modeller og detaljer op.
==PAGE:141==
D.4. INTERVIEW WITH VINCENT OLTHUIS - ABT CONSULTING ENGINEERS 123
D.4
Interview with Vincent Olthuis - abt Consulting
Engineers
Title: Building Engineer
Date: 23-Apr-2018
Every project will go trough changes, most common cause are assumptions in the early
design phase. This simply is the case, because you can’t calculate every element by
hand with the start of a project. During the length of a project things will get more
speciﬁc. Untill you’re sure about the things you drawn or calculated, things will change.
Besides this, architects will, troughout the proces, change there design to optimise it.
This will cause for changes also.
This is a diﬃcult question. I think this depents on the design team that works on the
project. They have a feeling of being involved in the project. When they see some
missteps in the design, they take a more critical look to the other part of the design.
(i’m not that experienced yet.)
When you decided too start a project with automation and optimalistion, the project
will be build up with less assumptions in the early stages. If you build the script the
right way, it can handle design changes, so you can optimize shape changes quite easily.
At the end of the project it will save time i think. The steps you could follow are:
Think, make the rules, code, hit Enter. The key steps in this proces are Think and
make the rules. The rules decide how ﬂexible and adaptive you’re workﬂow will be
and how many options you will end up with. The more results you get, the better
and weighted you’re decision will be, but the longer you’re computer has to calculate.
Thats something you have too consider before you start. When this is done right, you
can certainly make more informed decisions at an early stage.
I can’t really answer this my self. Im not a structural engineer, so this is kind of out of
my league. I asked some of my colleagues to help me with you’re interview questions.
In a traditional project, early changes are relative cheap and easy to process. The fur-
ther you get in the design stage, the more expensive and harder they are too process
and this will result in delay. In the automated design project, you have too consider
the rules good. When the rules are good, making changes in the design are quite easy.
When you need to change the rules (and so the script), you basically start from scratch
again.
How often does major project (design) changes take place after the early design phase,
and what are the most common cause for these changes?
Often, there are several common causes
- The design is not detailed enough in an earlier phase, requiring redesign.
- The client realizes what he needs when the design is “ﬁnished” and changes his
requirements.
- The design is costs too much and needs to be economized.
On what grounds are designs evaluated and “rated” during the early design?
That depends on the parties involved, each client/architect has their own issues they
==PAGE:142==
124
APPENDIX D. INTERVIEW ANSWERS
focus on.
How do you see the possibilities of using a semi-automated process of performing prelim-
inary analysis on early project proposals and thus open for the possibilities for making
more informed decisions at an early stage?
This might work as long as it doesn’t constrain the freedom of the parties to choose
their own process/workﬂow
It needs to be able to deal with diﬀerent ‘levels’ of design detailing as well as abstrac-
tions
To what degree does the models used in the early design phase contain relevant infor-
mation regarding key structural components?
Generally no deﬁnitive information, the structural engineer only indicates whether a
solution is viable.
How does the project change work-ﬂow look from your perspective from starting a pro-
cess to delivering a ﬁnished project, what are the most common changes etc.?
Economize, diﬀerent materials diﬀerent ﬁnishes
Redesigning around structural elements that are larger/diﬀerent than the assumptions
earlier in the process
Changes in the design that stem from applying detailing to all elements (conﬂicts,
impossible situations, better solutions)
